[32] Since I can get greatness of soul and nobility from myself, why should I look to get a farm, or money, or some office, from you? I will not be so insensible of what I already own.

[33] For men who are meek and cowardly, though, there is no option but to write letters for them as if they were already dead. ‚ÄòPlease grant us the body of so-and-so together with his meagre ration of blood.‚Äô [34] For, really, such a person amounts to no more than a carcass and a little blood. If he were anything more, he would realize that no one is ever unhappy because of someone else.

# 1.10 To those who have applied themselves to advancement at Rome ‚Üë

[1] If we philosophers had applied ourselves to our job as seriously as those old men in Rome pursue their interests, we, too, might be getting somewhere. [2] I have a friend even older than I who is now in charge of the grain supply in Rome. When he passed through here on his return from exile, what things he said in disparagement of his former life, swearing when he returned that from then on he would devote himself exclusively to a life of peace and tranquillity. ‚ÄòHow much time have I got left, after all?‚Äô

[3] And I said to him, ‚ÄòI don‚Äôt believe you. As soon as you get a whiff of Rome you will forget everything you‚Äôve said‚Äô ‚Äì and I added that if the least access to court became available to him, he would rush in, singing hymns of praise to the Almighty. [4] ‚ÄòListen, Epictetus,‚Äô he said, ‚Äòif you find me even putting a foot inside the court, feel free to think as little of me as you like.‚Äô [5] So what did he do? Before he even reached the limits of the capital he received letters from Caesar, immediately forgot all he‚Äôd said, and doesn‚Äôt seem to have given it a thought since. [6] I‚Äôd like to be there with him now to repeat to him the words he said when passing through here, and add, ‚ÄòHow much more shrewd a prophet I proved than you!‚Äô

[7] Well, am I implying that man is an animal unfit for action? Not at all. So why aren‚Äôt we more active? [8] I mean, look at me. When day begins, I remind myself of the author we are supposed to be reading; but then I think to myself, ‚ÄòWho cares how this or that student reads the author; first let me get my sleep!‚Äô

[9] And yet how can their business compare in importance to ours? If you could see them at Rome, you would find that they do nothing all day but vote on a resolution, then huddle together a while to deliberate about grain, land or some other means to make a living. [10] Is it the same thing to receive a petition that reads, ‚ÄòPlease allow me to export a bit of grain,‚Äô and ‚ÄòPlease learn from Chrysippus how the universe is governed, and what place the rational creature has in it; find out, too, who you are, and what constitutes your good and your evil‚Äô? [11] Is one to be compared with the other? Do they deserve the same degree of application? [12] Is it equally wrong to neglect this one as that?

Well, am I and the other teachers the only ones who are lazy and indifferent? [13] You young people are even worse. Old men like us, you know, when we see children at play, like nothing better than to join them in their game. Likewise, I would be a lot more excited about joining you in serious study if I were once to see you bright- eyed and enthusiastic.

# 1.11 Concerning family affection ‚Üë

[1] During a visit from a magistrate Epictetus, in the course of conversing, asked the man whether he had a wife and children. [2] He replied that he did. Epictetus then asked, ‚ÄòAnd how do you like it?‚Äô

‚ÄòI‚Äôm miserable,‚Äô he said.

So Epictetus asked, ‚ÄòHow so? [3] Men don‚Äôt marry and have children in order to be miserable, but to be happy.‚Äô

[4] ‚ÄòI‚Äôm so anxious about my poor children,‚Äô the man said, ‚Äòthat the other day, when my little daughter was sick and appeared in danger of her life, I could not bear even to remain with her. I had to leave her side and go off until word was brought me that her condition had improved.‚Äô

‚ÄòWell,‚Äô said Epictetus, ‚Äòdo you think you acted correctly in this case?‚Äô

[5] ‚ÄòI acted naturally,‚Äô he answered.

‚ÄòIf you can convince me that you acted naturally, I am ready on my side to show that anything done in accord with nature is done correctly.‚Äô

[6] ‚ÄòIt‚Äôs what nearly all we fathers go through.‚Äô

‚ÄòI don‚Äôt dispute that reactions like yours occur,‚Äô Epictetus said. ‚ÄòThe point at issue between us is whether they ought to. [7] For by your reasoning we must allow that tumours happen for the good of the body, because they happen, and that doing wrong is in accord with nature, because all, or at least most of us, do wrong. [8] So show me how, exactly, you acted in accordance with nature.‚Äô

‚ÄòI don‚Äôt think I can,‚Äô the man said. ‚ÄòWhy don‚Äôt you show me instead how it is not in accordance with nature, and shouldn‚Äôt happen?‚Äô

So Epictetus said, [9] ‚ÄòWell, if we were in any uncertainty about white and black things, what criterion would we adduce to choose between them?‚Äô

‚ÄòSight,‚Äô the man said.

‚ÄòAnd what about things hot and cold, or hard and soft -what would decide in those cases?‚Äô

‚ÄòTouch.‚Äô

[10] ‚ÄòNow, since we have a difference of opinion about what is in agreement with nature and what is right and wrong, what standard should we apply here, do you think?‚Äô

‚ÄòI don‚Äôt know,‚Äô the man said.

[11] ‚ÄòWell, ignorance in discriminating between colours, smells or flavours probably does no great harm. But not to know about right and wrong, about what is natural in man‚Äôs case and what is not ‚Äì is that a minor shortcoming, do you think?‚Äô

‚ÄòNo, a very great one, I admit.‚Äô

[12] ‚ÄòConsider now ‚Äì is everything that people judge to be good and appropriate rightly judged so? Can Jews, Syrians, Egyptians and Romans all be right in the opinions they have about food, for example?‚Äô18

‚ÄòHow could they?‚Äô

[13] ‚ÄòInstead, if the opinions of the Egyptians are right, I suppose that the others are wrong. Or if the Jews are right, then the others can‚Äôt be.‚Äô

‚ÄòNo, they cannot.‚Äô

[14] ‚ÄòAnd where there is ignorance, there is also want of learning and instruction in essentials.‚Äô

The man agreed.

[15] ‚ÄòOnce you‚Äôve realized this, you will occupy your mind and devote all your attention toward finding that standard that discriminates between what is natural and what is not; and then apply it to particular cases as they arise.

[16] ‚ÄòFor the present, I can only offer the following by way of assistance toward solving our problem. [17] Tell me, then, do you think family affection is good and agrees with nature‚Äôs norms?‚Äô

‚ÄòI do.‚Äô

‚ÄòCan family affection be good and natural, while what agrees with reason is not good?‚Äô

‚ÄòOf course not.‚Äô

[18] ‚ÄòSo whatever is rational will not be in conflict with family affection.‚Äô

‚ÄòI suppose not.‚Äô

‚ÄòBecause, if they were, one would be in agreement with nature while the other would have to conflict.‚Äô

‚ÄòCorrect.‚Äô

[19] ‚ÄòSo if there‚Äôs anything out there that‚Äôs both affectionate and rational, this we can safely say is also both right and good.‚Äô

‚ÄòAgreed.‚Äô

[20] ‚ÄòNow, to leave your child‚Äôs side when she is sick, and go away, is not a rational act, and I don‚Äôt suppose that even you will argue otherwise. But we still have to consider whether it is consistent with family affection.‚Äô

‚ÄòThen let‚Äôs do so.‚Äô

[21] ‚ÄòWas it right for you, being affectionately disposed toward your child, to go off and leave her? Let‚Äôs take her mother instead. Doesn‚Äôt she feel affection for her daughter?‚Äô

[22] ‚ÄòOf course she does.‚Äô

‚ÄòThen should she, too, have left her?‚Äô

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

‚ÄòAnd the nurse ‚Äì does she have affectionate feelings toward the girl?‚Äô

‚ÄòShe does.‚Äô

‚ÄòSo should she, also, have left her?‚Äô

‚ÄòCertainly not.‚Äô

‚ÄòAnd her personal slave and teacher, doesn‚Äôt he have feelings for her?‚Äô

‚ÄòYes.‚Äô

[23] ‚ÄòI suppose that means that he, too, should have gone off and left her ‚Äì the result being that, owing to this very great affection on the part of parents as well as guardians, the girl would have been completely forsaken by those who love and protect her, to die in the company of people who had no part in bringing her up, and therefore no special feelings for her.‚Äô

‚ÄòI can hardly suppose so.‚Äô

[24] ‚ÄòIn fact, it is unfair and illogical to say that people whose affection is the equal of yours should not be permitted to do what you claim was justified in your case owing to this very great affection you profess.‚Äô

‚ÄòTrue enough.‚Äô

[25] ‚ÄòI mean, if you were sick, would you want your family, your wife, children and the rest, to be so caring as to walk away from you and leave you to yourself?‚Äô

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

[26] ‚ÄòAnd would you want to be so loved by them that, because of their love, you would always suffer sickness in isolation? Isn‚Äôt that more like the affection you pray your enemies would show you, that they should go away and leave you be? And if so, the inescapable conclusion is that what you did was no act of affection at all.

[27] ‚ÄòWell, it had to have been something that affected you so much that you walked out on your child. It might be related to the impulse that made a man at Rome cover his head while his favourite horse was running, and who later required sponges to be revived when his horse unexpectedly won. [28] The precise explanation for such behaviour might be out of place here. For now, assuming that philosophy contains at least a grain of truth, it‚Äôs enough to be convinced that you will not find it by looking for any cause outside yourself. The same thing is always the reason for our doing or not doing something, for saying or not saying something, for being elated or depressed, for going after something or avoiding it. [29] It‚Äôs the same reason that you‚Äôre here now listening to me, and I‚Äôm saying the things that I‚Äôm now saying ‚Äì [30] our opinion that all these things are right.‚Äô

‚ÄòOf course.‚Äô

‚ÄòIf we saw things differently we would act differently, in line with our different idea of what is right and wrong. [31] This, then, was the cause of Achilles‚Äô lamentations ‚Äì not the fact that Patroclus died, since other people don‚Äôt carry on so when a friend or companion dies ‚Äì but the fact that he chose to lament. [32] The same cause lies behind your desertion of your daughter ‚Äì you thought it was a good idea at the time. Conversely, if you stay with her, it would be for the same reason. Now you are about to return to Rome; that is your decision ‚Äì but if it changes, you won‚Äôt go. [33] In other words, it isn‚Äôt death, pain, exile or anything else you care to mention that accounts for the way we act, only our opinion about death, pain and the rest.

[34] ‚ÄòWell, have I convinced you of this, or not?‚Äô

‚ÄòYou have,‚Äô the man said.

‚ÄòAs the cause is, so is the result. [35] Whenever we do something wrong, then, from now on we will not blame anything except the opinion on which it‚Äôs based; and we will try to root out wrong opinions with more determination than we remove tumours or infections from the body. [36] By the same token, we will acknowledge opinion as the source of our good behaviour too. [37] But wife, child, slave or neighbour ‚Äì in the future we won‚Äôt name any of them as authors of the evil in our lives, in the knowledge that, unless we judge things in a particular light, we won‚Äôt act in the corresponding manner. And we, not externals, are the masters of our judgements.‚Äô

‚ÄòAgreed.‚Äô

[38] ‚ÄòSo, starting today, we won‚Äôt trouble to assess or analyse land, slaves, horses or dogs as to their quality or condition -only our opinions.‚Äô

‚ÄòWell, I hope so,‚Äô the man said.

[39] ‚ÄòYou see, you are going to have to become a student again ‚Äì that universal figure of fun ‚Äì if you really mean to subject your opinions to honest examination. And you know as well as I do that this assignment can‚Äôt be completed overnight.‚Äô

# 1.12 On satisfaction ‚Üë

[1] On the subject of the gods, there are those who deny the existence of divinity outright. Others say that God exists, but is idle and indifferent and does not pay attention to anything. [2] A third group says that God exists and is attentive, but only to the workings of the heavens, never affairs on earth. A fourth group says that he does attend to earthly affairs, including the welfare of humanity, but only in a general way, without worrying about individuals. [3] And then there is a fifth group, Odysseus and Socrates among them, who say that ‚ÄòI cannot make a move without God‚Äôs notice.‚Äô19

[4] Before doing anything else we need to examine these views separately to decide which are true and false. [5] Because if the gods do not exist, what sense can be made of the command to ‚Äòfollow the gods‚Äô?20 And how can it be a sensible goal if they exist, but do not have any cares? [6] Even supposing that they exist and care, if that care does not extend to people, and, in point of fact, to me personally, it is still no worthwhile goal.

[7] The intelligent person, after due consideration of the question, will decide to submit his will to the ruler of the universe, as good citizens submit to the laws of the state.

[8] Education should be approached with this goal in mind: ‚ÄòHow can I personally follow the gods always, and how can I adapt to God‚Äôs government, and so be free?‚Äô [9] Freedom, you see, is having events go in accordance with our will, never contrary to it.

[10] Well ‚Äì is freedom the same as madness? Of course not. Madness and freedom are poles apart. [11] ‚ÄòBut I want my wishes realized, never mind the reason behind them.‚Äô [12] Now, that‚Äôs madness, that‚Äôs insanity. Freedom is something good and valuable; to arbitrarily wish for things to happen that arbitrarily seem to you best is not good, it‚Äôs disgraceful.

How do we approach the practice of writing? [13] Do I want to write the name ‚ÄòDion‚Äô whatever way I please? No, I learn to want to write it the way it is supposed to be written. The case is the same with music, [14] the same with every art and science; it would not be worth the trouble to learn them, otherwise, if they accommodated everyone‚Äôs wishes. [15] And freedom, the greatest possession of all, is the last thing you would expect to be different, where wishes are given carte blanche. Getting an education means learning to bring our will in line with the way things happen ‚Äì which is to say, as the ruler of the universe arranged. [16] He arranged for there to be summer and winter, abundance and lack, virtue and vice ‚Äì all such opposites meant for the harmony of the whole; and he gave us each a body and bodily parts, material belongings, family and friends.21

[17] It is with this arrangement in mind that we should approach instruction, not to alter the facts ‚Äì since this is neither allowed, nor is it better that it should be ‚Äì but in order to learn the nature of what concerns us, and keep our will in line with events. [18] Can we avoid people? How is that possible? And if we associate with them, can we change them? Who gives us that power? [19] What is the alternative ‚Äì what means can be found for dealing with them? One that ensures that we remain true to our nature, however other people see fit to behave. [20] That‚Äôs not what you do, though. No, you gripe and protest against circumstance. If you‚Äôre alone, you call it desolation, if you‚Äôre in company you describe them all as swindlers and backstabbers; you curse your own parents, your children, your siblings and neighbours. [21] When you are by yourself you should call it peace and liberty, and consider yourself the gods‚Äô equal. When you‚Äôre with a large group you shouldn‚Äôt say you‚Äôre in a mob or crowd, but a guest at a feast or festival ‚Äì and in that spirit learn to enjoy it.

What is the downside for those who refuse to accept it? To be just as they are. [22] Is someone unhappy being alone? Leave him to his isolation. Is someone unhappy with his parents? Let him be a bad son, and grumble. Is someone unhappy with his children? Let him be a bad father. [23] ‚ÄòThrow him in jail.‚Äô What jail? The one he is in already, since he is there against his will; and if he is there against his will then he is imprisoned. Conversely, Socrates was not in prison because he chose to be there.22

[24] ‚ÄòBut my leg is crippled.‚Äô

Slave, are you going to be at odds with the world because of one lame leg? Shouldn‚Äôt you rather make the world a gift of it, and gladly return it to the one who gave it to you originally? [25] Are you going to make Zeus your enemy, and set your face against the Fates, with whom Zeus spun the thread of your destiny at the moment you were born, laying out his plans for you?

[26] You ought to realize, you take up very little space in the world as a whole ‚Äì your body, that is; in reason, however, you yield to no one, not even to the gods, because reason is not measured in size but sense. [27] So why not care for that side of you, where you and the gods are equals?

[28] ‚ÄòIt‚Äôs my bad luck to have awful parents.‚Äô

Well, you couldn‚Äôt very well choose them beforehand, saying, ‚ÄòLet this man have intercourse with this woman, at this particular, so that I can be conceived.‚Äô [29] Your parents had to come first, then you had to be born the way you are, of parents the way they are.

[30] Does that mean you have to be miserable? Let‚Äôs suppose you didn‚Äôt understand what you had the power of vision for; it would be your bad luck if you decided to close your eyes just at the moment a beautiful painting passed before you. You are even unluckier for being oblivious to the fact that you have the power of patience to deal with your diffculties. [31] You forget the virtues of character you have in reserve, just when problems that they can control present themselves, and you could use their help.

[32] You should thank the gods for making you strong enough to survive what you cannot control, and only responsible for what you can. [33] The gods have released you from accountability for your parents, your siblings, your body, your possessions ‚Äì for death and for life itself. [34] They made you responsible only for what is in your power ‚Äì the proper use of impressions. [35] So why take on the burden of matters which you cannot answer for? You are only making unnecessary problems for yourself.

# 1.13 On the treatment of slaves ‚Üë

[1] When someone asked how it was possible to eat in a manner pleasing to the gods, Epictetus said, ‚ÄòIf is done justly and equitably, in moderation, with restraint and self-control ‚Äì isn‚Äôt that pleasing to the gods?

[2] ‚ÄòWhen you ask for hot water and the slave ignores you; or if he hears you but brings in water that is barely lukewarm; or if he is not even to be found in the house ‚Äì to refrain from anger and not explode in that situation, won‚Äôt that find favour with the gods?‚Äô

[3] ‚ÄòBut how are we to bear with such slaves?‚Äô

My friend, it‚Äôs a matter of bearing with your own brother, who has Zeus as his ancestor and is a son born of the same seed as yourself, with the same high lineage.

[4] ‚ÄòIf you have been placed in a position above others, are you automatically going to behave like a despot? Remember who you are and whom you govern ‚Äì that they are kinsmen, brothers by nature, fellow descendants of Zeus.‚Äô

[5] ‚ÄòBut I have a bill of sale for them, while they have none for me.‚Äô

‚ÄòDon‚Äôt you see that, instead of the laws of the gods, you only have regard for the earth, the pit and the contemptible laws of the dead?‚Äô

# 1.14 That God supervises everyone ‚Üë

[1] Someone asked Epictetus how a person could be convinced that everything he did was supervised by God.

‚ÄòDon‚Äôt you believe,‚Äô he answered, ‚Äòthat all things are united as one?‚Äô

‚ÄòI do,‚Äô the other said.

[2] ‚ÄòWell, don‚Äôt you think that there is interaction between things on earth and the things in heaven?‚Äô

‚ÄòYes.‚Äô

[3] ‚ÄòHow else, after all, could things take place with such regularity, as if God were issuing orders? When he tells plants to bloom, they bloom, when he tells them to bear fruit, they bear fruit, when he tells them to ripen, they ripen. Similarly, when he tells them to drop their fruit, shed their leaves, draw in and lie dormant for the winter, all that they do too. [4] How else explain that the waxing and the waning of the moon, and the coming and going of the sun, coincide with such obvious changes and fluctuations here on earth?

[5] ‚ÄòWell, if plants and our bodies are so intimately linked to the world and its rhythms, won‚Äôt the same be true of our minds ‚Äì only more so? [6] And if our minds are so intimately connected with God as to be divine sparks of his being, is he not going to perceive their every movement, since the parts in motion participate in his nature?

[7] Now you, for your part, are capable of reflecting in detail on God and his government, while also tending to human affairs. You can process in your intellect and senses a wealth of thoughts and impressions simultaneously. There are impressions that you assent to, others that you reject; sometimes you suspend judgement altogether. [8] Your mind can store so many of these impressions and from a wide range of sources too. Under their influence, the mind proceeds to form ideas that correspond with particular impressions. That‚Äôs how we form memories, and how the many varieties of art and science are created.

[9] ‚ÄòAnd now God ‚Äì are we going to deny him the ability to oversee everything that happens, even when we know that he is present everywhere and enjoys a kind of communion with the world?

[10] ‚ÄòThe sun is capable of illuminating a large part of the universe, just leaving unlit the small shadow that the earth casts.23 Well, here is God, who created the sun (which is minuscule in relation to the whole universe), and who still causes the sun to turn ‚Äì wouldn‚Äôt you say he is in a good position to see everything that goes on?‚Äô

[11] ‚ÄòAnd yet,‚Äô someone objects, ‚ÄòI cannot understand all these things at one and the same time.‚Äô

‚ÄòIs anyone saying that your capacities are the equal of God‚Äôs? [12] Nevertheless, he has provided each of us with an individual guardian deity, which stays by our side and is in charge of looking after us ‚Äì a guardian who never sleeps and is impossible to distract. [13] Is there any guardian to whose care he could have committed us that is better or more vigilant? Whenever you close your doors and turn out your lights, remember, never say to yourself that you are alone; [14] you‚Äôre not. God is inside, and so is your private deity; and neither of them requires light to watch you by.

[15] ‚ÄòThis is the deity who deserves your pledge of allegiance, as soldiers swear before Caesar. If they want to be paid, they must swear to put the emperor‚Äôs safety first. You, however, who have been chosen to receive an abundance of blessings ‚Äì and for free ‚Äì why won‚Äôt you swear a similar oath, and, if you have done so already, why not reaffirm the commitment?

[16] ‚ÄòWhat is that oath? You swear that under no circumstances will you disobey, press charges, or find fault with God and his gifts. You won‚Äôt shrink from life‚Äôs essential tasks or trials. [17] Can anyone seriously compare the soldier‚Äôs oath with ours? They swear to honour no one more than Caesar; we swear to honour no one more than ourselves.‚Äô

# 1.15 What philosophy professes ‚Üë

[1] Someone asked Epictetus‚Äô advice on how to get his brother to stop being on bad terms with him. Epictetus said, [2] ‚ÄòPhilosophy does not claim to secure for us anything outside our control.

Otherwise it would be taking on matters that do not concern it. For as wood is the material of the carpenter, and marble that of the sculptor, so the subject matter of the art of life is the life of the self.‚Äô

[3] ‚ÄòWell, what about my brother‚Äôs life?‚Äô

‚ÄòThat, in turn, belongs to his own art of living. With respect to yours, it is one of the externals ‚Äì no different from land, health or reputation; and philosophy makes no promises concerning those. [4] In every situation I will keep the soul‚Äôs governing principle in agreement with nature.‚Äô

‚ÄòWhose governing principle?‚Äô

‚ÄòOnly the one inside me.‚Äô

[5] ‚ÄòHow, then, do I stop my brother from being angry with me?‚Äô

‚ÄòBring him to me and I will speak with him; but about his anger I have nothing to say to you.‚Äô

[6] The man consulting with Epictetus then asked, ‚ÄòWhat I want to know is this, then: how can I stay true to nature even if my brother won‚Äôt reconcile with me?‚Äô

[7] ‚ÄòNothing important comes into being overnight; even grapes or figs need time to ripen. If you say that you want a fig now, I will tell you to be patient. First, you must allow the tree to flower, then put forth fruit; then you have to wait until the fruit is ripe. [8] So if the fruit of a fig tree is not brought to maturity instantly or in an hour, how do you expect the human mind to come to fruition, so quickly and easily? Don‚Äôt expect it, even if I were personally to tell you it was possible.‚Äô

# 1.16 On providence ‚Üë

[1] Do not be surprised if other animals have their biological necessities provided for them ‚Äì not just food and drink, but a place to sleep ‚Äì and have no need of shoes, bedding or clothes, while humans, on the other hand, need all these things. [2] It was not expedient in the case of creatures born to serve others, not themselves, to have made them in any way deficient in these respects. [3] After all, think what it would be like if we had to worry about finding clothes and shoes, or food and drink, for our sheep and asses, in addition to having to provide these essentials for ourselves.

[4] Just as soldiers report to their commander at the ready, clothed, armed, and shod ‚Äì and it would be too much to expect the commander to have to equip them out of his own time and pocket ‚Äì so nature has created the animals that are born for service ready for our use and in no need of further attention. [5] The result is that a single small child, armed with no more than a stick, can control an entire flock of sheep.

[6] As it is, though, we neglect to give thanks for not having to bestow on beasts the same care as we do on ourselves, then complain to God concerning our own condition. [7] Yet, I swear, if we had an ounce of gratitude or respect, the least aspect of creation would be enough to get us to admit the existence of providence. [8] And I‚Äôm not talking about great things, now; take the mere fact that milk is produced from grass, and that cheese comes from milk, and wool can grow out of skin. Who is it that has created or conceived these things? ‚ÄòNo one,‚Äô people say. What ignorance and gall!

[9] Let‚Äôs leave the chief features of nature aside, and just consider its secondary effects. [10] Is there anything of less utility than a beard? But nature has found a most becoming use even for that, enabling us to discriminate between the man and woman. [11] Nature identifies itself even at a distance: ‚ÄòI am a man: come and deal with me on these terms. Nothing else is needed; just take note of nature‚Äôs signs.‚Äô

[12] In the case of woman, in addition to mingling a softer note in her voice, nature has removed the hair from her chin. But you say, ‚ÄòThe creature could have done very well without this distinguishing mark; we could announce of ourselves that we are man or woman.‚Äô [13] But you miss the point: look how proud, handsome and becoming the sign is, much more attractive than the cock‚Äôs comb, prouder even than the lion‚Äôs mane. [14] That is why we should safeguard the signs that God gave us and by virtue of which the genders were intended to be distinguished.

[15] Are these the only works of providence relevant in our case? Hardly. In fact, there are no words adequate to praise them or do them justice. If we had sense, there is nothing better we could do with our time than praise God and proclaim his good works, whether in public or private. [16] We should praise him even while we‚Äôre busy digging, ploughing or eating. ‚ÄòGod is great ‚Äì he has given us these instruments to work the earth. [17] God is great ‚Äì he has given us hands, a mouth and a stomach, the ability to grow unconsciously, and to breathe while we‚Äôre asleep.‚Äô [18] This is what we ought to sing on every occasion ‚Äì more especially the greatest and holiest hymn, celebrating the ability he bestowed on us to understand his works, and use them systematically.

[19] Well, since most of you are blind, I suppose there has to be someone who fills this role, and will praise God on others‚Äô behalf. [20] And what is a lame old man like myself good for, anyway, except singing God‚Äôs praises? If I were a nightingale or a swan, I would sing the song either of them was born to sing. But I am a rational being, so my song must take the form of a hymn. [21] That is my job, which I will keep to as long as I am permitted; and I invite any and all of you to join me.

# 1.17 Concerning the necessity of logic ‚Üë

[1] Since reason is what analyses and coordinates everything, it should not go itself unanalysed. Then what will it be analysed by?

[2] Obviously by itself or something different. Now, this something different must either be reason or something superior to reason ‚Äì which is impossible, since there is nothing superior to reason. [3] But if it is analysed by reason, what, in turn, will analyse that reason? Itself? If that‚Äôs the case, however, the first occurrence of reason could have done the same; whereas if another form of reason is required, the process will continue for ever.

[4] ‚ÄòYes, but in any case it is more important that we tend to our passions, and our opinions, and the like.‚Äô

So it is lectures about these subjects that you‚Äôd rather hear? Fine. [5] But if you say to me, ‚ÄòI don‚Äôt know whether your arguments are sound or not,‚Äô or if I use a word in an ambiguous sense and you ask me to ‚ÄòPlease distinguish‚Äô ‚Äì well, I‚Äôm not going to be in a mood to oblige you, I‚Äôm more liable to say, ‚ÄòIt‚Äôs more important that we tend to our passions and our opinions, and the like.‚Äô

[6] Which, I suppose, is why Stoics put logic at the head of our curriculum ‚Äì for the same reason that, before a quantity of grain can be measured, we must settle on a standard of measurement. [7] If we don‚Äôt begin by establishing standards of weight and volume, how are we going to measure or weigh anything? [8] And similarly in the present case ‚Äì if we haven‚Äôt fully grasped and refined the instrument by which we analyse and understand other things, how can we hope to understand them with any precision?

[9] ‚ÄòBut a measuring bowl is a mere thing of wood, and doesn‚Äôt put forth fruit.‚Äô

It measures grain, however.

[10] ‚ÄòMatters of logic are unproductive as well.‚Äô

Well, we will see about that. But even if you are right, because it analyses and distinguishes them logic functions, in a way, as the weight and measure for abstract matters, and that is reason enough, the experts say, to study it. [11] Who says so? Besides Chrysippus, Zeno and Cleanthes, I mean?24 [12] How about Antisthenes? It was he who wrote, ‚ÄòThe beginning of education is the examination of terms.‚Äô And as we know from Xenophon, Socrates routinely began his talks by analysing terms, in order to forestall any uncertainty as to their meaning.

[13] Is this the ultimate achievement, then ‚Äì getting to where we can understand and interpret Chrysippus? Nobody is saying that. [14] Well, what is, then? Understanding the will of nature. Yes, but can you understand it alone and unaided? Evidently you could still use help; because if it is true that we always err unwillingly,25 and if you were already in possession of the truth, it would be reflected in your flawless behaviour.

[15] But I don‚Äôt understand the will of nature. Who will explain it to me? I‚Äôm told Chrysippus can. [16] So off I go to find out what this interpreter of nature has to say. But then there‚Äôs a passage I have trouble with, so I cast about for someone to explain the explanation. ‚ÄòHere,‚Äô I say, ‚Äòhave a look and tell me what this means‚Äô ‚Äì as if the thing were in Latin. [17] What right has the commentator to feel superior? Even Chrysippus has no right to be proud if he only explains the will of nature and does not follow it; how much less entitled to pride is his interpreter!

[18] It‚Äôs not Chrysippus per se we need, but only insofar as he helps us understand nature. We don‚Äôt need the prophet for his own sake, but through him we think we can divine the future and better understand the signs that the gods send. [19] We don‚Äôt need the victim‚Äôs entrails for their own sake, only for the sake of the signs they convey. And we don‚Äôt worship the crow or the raven ‚Äì we worship God, who communicates by means of them. [20] Now imagine me before a priest or prophet, to whom I say, ‚ÄòPlease examine this victim‚Äôs entrails; what are they telling me?‚Äô [21] After carefully spreading them out, he announces, ‚ÄòIt‚Äôs written here that you have a will incapable of being coerced or compelled. [22] Start with assent: can anyone prevent you from agreeing with what is true?‚Äô

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

‚ÄòOr force you into believing what is false?‚Äô

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

[23] ‚ÄòPlainly, then, in this area your will cannot be hindered, forced or obstructed. [24] Let‚Äôs look at desire and impulse: you see it‚Äôs just the same. One impulse can only be overruled by another.‚Äô

[25] ‚ÄòWhat about if someone threatens me with death, though; surely he compels me then?‚Äô

‚ÄòIt isn‚Äôt what you‚Äôre threatened with ‚Äì it‚Äôs the fact that you prefer to do anything rather than die. [26] It‚Äôs your set of values that compelled you: will acting on will. [27] If God had made it possible for the fragment of his own being that he gave us to be hindered or coerced by anyone ‚Äì himself included ‚Äì then he wouldn‚Äôt be God, and wouldn‚Äôt be looking after us the way a god ought to. [28] ‚ÄúThat,‚Äù the priest says, ‚Äúis what I find inscribed in the sacrifice. This is God‚Äôs signal to you: if you want, you are free; if you want, you will blame no one, you will accuse no one ‚Äì if you want, everything will happen according to plan, yours as well as God‚Äôs.‚Äù

[29] ‚ÄòThat‚Äôs the kind of prophecy I go to this priest for, and to the philosopher; out of reverence, not for him, but for his powers of interpretation.‚Äô

# 1.18 Don‚Äôt be angry with wrongdoers ‚Üë

[1] Philosophers say that people are all guided by a single standard. When they assent to a thing, it is because they feel it must be true, when they dissent, it is because they feel something isn‚Äôt true, and when they suspend judgement, it is because they feel that the thing is unclear. [2] Similarly, they say that in the case of impulse people feel that its object must be to their advantage, and that it is impossible to consider any one thing advantageous and desire something different, or consider one thing right and have an impulse to do something else.

If all this is true, then what grounds do we have for being angry with anyone? [3] We use labels like ‚Äòthief‚Äô and ‚Äòrobber‚Äô in connection with them, but what do these words mean? They merely signify that people are confused about what is good and what is bad. So should we be angry with them, or should we pity them instead? [4] Show them where they go wrong and you will find that they‚Äôll reform. But unless they see it, they are stuck with nothing better than their usual opinion as their practical guide.

[5] ‚ÄòWell, shouldn‚Äôt we do away with thieves and degenerates?‚Äô

Try putting the question this way: [6] ‚ÄòShouldn‚Äôt we rid ourselves of people deceived about what‚Äôs most important, people who are blind ‚Äì not in their faculty of vision, their ability to distinguish white from black ‚Äì but in the moral capacity to distinguish good from bad?‚Äô [7] Put it that way, and you‚Äôll realize how inhumane your position is. It is as if you were to say, ‚ÄòShouldn‚Äôt this blind man, and this deaf man, be executed?‚Äô [8] Because if loss of the greatest asset involves the greatest harm, and someone is deprived of their moral bearings, which is the most important capacity they have ‚Äì well, why add anger to their loss? [9] If you must be affected by other people‚Äôs misfortunes, show them pity instead of contempt. Drop this readiness to hate and take offence. [10] Who are you to use those common curses, like ‚ÄòThese damned fools,‚Äô etc.? [11] Let them be. Since when are you so intelligent as to go around correcting other people‚Äôs mistakes? We get angry because we put too high a premium on things that they can steal. Don‚Äôt attach such value to your clothes, and you won‚Äôt get angry with the thief who takes them. Don‚Äôt make your wife‚Äôs external beauty her chief attraction, and you won‚Äôt be angry with the adulterer. [12] Realize that the thief and the adulterer cannot touch what‚Äôs yours, only what is common property everywhere and not under your control. If you make light of those things and ignore them, who is left to be angry with? As long as you honour material things, direct your anger at yourself rather than the thief or adulterer.

[13] Look at it this way. You have beautiful clothes and your neighbour does not. You have a window and want to give them an airing. The neighbour does not know what man‚Äôs good consists in, but imagines it means having beautiful clothes ‚Äì the opinion you happen to share. [14] It‚Äôs a foregone conclusion that he‚Äôs going to try and steal them. I mean, when starving people see you gobbling down food all by yourself, you know one of them will make a grab at it. So don‚Äôt provoke them ‚Äì don‚Äôt air your clothes at the window!

[15] Something similar happened to me the other day. I keep an iron lamp by my household shrine. Hearing a noise from my window, I ran down and found the lamp had been lifted. I reasoned that the thief who took it must have felt an impulse he couldn‚Äôt resist. So I said to myself, ‚ÄòTomorrow you‚Äôll get a cheaper, less attractive one made of clay.‚Äô [16] A man only loses what he has. ‚ÄòI lost clothes.‚Äô Yes, because you had clothes. ‚ÄòI have a pain in the head.‚Äô Well, at least you don‚Äôt have a pain in the horns, right? Loss and sorrow are only possible with respect to things we own.

[17] ‚ÄòBut the tyrant will chain -‚Äô What will he chain? Your leg. ‚ÄòHe will chop off -‚Äô What? Your head. What he will never chain or chop off is your integrity. That‚Äôs the reason behind the ancient advice to ‚Äòknow yourself‚Äô.

[18] We should discipline ourselves in small things, and from there progress to things of greater value. [19] If you have a headache, practise not cursing. Don‚Äôt curse every time you have an earache. And I‚Äôm not saying that you can‚Äôt complain, only don‚Äôt complain with your whole being. If your servant is slow to bring you a bandage, don‚Äôt roll around and yell, ‚ÄòEverybody hates me!‚Äô Who wouldn‚Äôt hate such a person? [20] Walk upright and free, trusting in the strength of your moral convictions, not the strength of your body, like an athlete. You weren‚Äôt meant to be invincible by brute force, like a pack animal. [21] You are invincible if nothing outside the will can disconcert you.

So I run through every scenario and consider them as an athlete might: ‚ÄòHe lasted the first round; how will he do in the second? [22] What if it‚Äôs hot? What if it‚Äôs the Olympics?‚Äô Similarly: ‚ÄòIf you entice him with money, he will turn up his nose. But what if it‚Äôs a pretty girl ‚Äì whom he meets in the dark? What if you tempt him with fame? Or test him with censure ‚Äì or applause? Or death?‚Äô All these he can handle. [23] But what if it‚Äôs really sweltering ‚Äì that is, what if he‚Äôs drunk? Or delirious? Or dreaming?26 If he can come through safely under all these conditions ‚Äì well, that‚Äôs the invincible ‚Äòathlete‚Äô so far as I am concerned.

# 1.19 How we should act towards the powerful ‚Üë

[1] A person who enjoys some advantage, or just believes they do, will invariably grow to be arrogant, especially if they are uneducated. [2] The tyrant, for example, will say to you, ‚ÄòMy power is supreme.‚Äô

‚ÄòWill you do something for me then? I want uncurbed desire. Do you even have it to give? I want my aversion error-free. Do you have that? How about a faculty of impulse that is faultless? [3] No, you have no connection to that. Look, you entrust yourself to the pilot‚Äôs expertise when on board ship, and to the superior skill of the driver when you are in a carriage. [4] It‚Äôs no different with other skills. So what does your advantage amount to?‚Äô

‚ÄòEverybody gives me their attention and respect.‚Äô

‚ÄòRight, and I pay attention to my blackboard, wiping it, and washing it; and for my oil flask I‚Äôll even drive a nail in the wall. Does that make these things better than me? No ‚Äì it just means that they are useful to me somehow. I look after my horse too, [5] I wash its feet and brush its coat. The fact is, everyone looks after themselves; if they curry favour with you it‚Äôs as if they‚Äôre currying their horse. Who is there who respects you as a human being? [6] Who wants to be like you, to emulate you the way people emulated Socrates?‚Äô

‚ÄòBut I can cut off your head.‚Äô

‚ÄòGood point; I had forgotten that I should look out for you as I would look out for some virus or infection, and erect an altar to you on the model of the Altar of Fever at Rome.27

[7] ‚ÄòWhat frightens most people and keeps them subdued? It can‚Äôt be the tyrant and his bodyguards; what nature has made free can only be disturbed or hampered by itself. [8] A person‚Äôs own thoughts unnerve them. If a tyrant threatens to chain our leg, whoever holds his leg in high regard will beg for mercy, whereas the person who cares more for his character will answer back, ‚ÄúGo ahead and chain it, if that‚Äôs what you want.‚Äù‚Äô

‚ÄòAnd you don‚Äôt care?‚Äô

‚ÄòI don‚Äôt care.‚Äô

‚ÄòJust wait, I‚Äôll show you who‚Äôs in charge!‚Äô

[9] ‚ÄòHow do you propose to do that? Zeus himself has given me my freedom; he was not going to allow any son of his to be enslaved. You are master of my corpse, come help yourself to that.‚Äô

[10] ‚ÄòWhat about when you petition me for a favour, isn‚Äôt that proof of your regard?‚Äô

‚ÄòNo, it‚Äôs me looking after myself. If you press the point, I will concede that in the process I give you the same attention I give my dishes.

[11] ‚ÄòLook, this is not selfishness, it‚Äôs the nature of the beast; everything we do is done for our own ends. The sun moves across the sky for its own ends.28 Even Zeus acts for his own aims. [12] But when Zeus wants to be ‚ÄúRain-Bringer‚Äù, or ‚ÄúGrain-Giver‚Äù, or ‚ÄúFather of Gods and Men‚Äù, it‚Äôs obvious that he can only gain his goals and earn his epithets by doing some benefit for the world at large. [13] In the same way he made the rational animal, man, incapable of attaining any of his private ends without at the same time providing for the community.

[14] ‚ÄòThe upshot is that it is not anti-social to be constantly acting in one own‚Äôs self-interest. [15] We do not expect someone, after all, to be indifferent regarding himself and his welfare. It‚Äôs the basis of our principle of appropriation,29 the instinct that drives everyone‚Äôs behaviour. [16] Consequently when people are mistaken in the views they hold about things outside the will ‚Äì thinking that they are good or evil ‚Äì they naturally are going to grovel before tyrants.

[17] And if only it ended there! But they grovel before the tyrant‚Äôs lackies too. Tell me, how do underlings suddenly become sages when the emperor elevates them to the post of bathroom attendant? Why are we suddenly saying ‚ÄúFelicio‚Äôs30 advice to me was very astute.‚Äù [18] I hope he gets kicked out of the toilet, so I can see you change your mind again and declare publicly that he‚Äôs a fool.

[19] ‚ÄòEpaphroditus once owned a slave, a shoemaker, whom he sold because he was no good. As chance would have it, he was bought by one of the imperial household and became shoemaker to Caesar. You should have seen Epaphroditus flatter him then! [20] ‚ÄúAnd how is my friend Felicio today?‚Äù [21] Whenever one of us asked, ‚ÄúWhere is the master?‚Äù he would be told, ‚ÄúHe is in conference with Felicio.‚Äù [22] Hadn‚Äôt he sold him off because he was useless? [23] How did he become so knowledgeable all of a sudden? Well, that‚Äôs what comes of valuing anything not under control of the will. [24] Someone is raised to the office of tribune and accepts congratulations on every hand. One person kisses his eyes, another his cheek, his slaves even kiss his hands. When he gets home, he finds lamps being lit in his honour. [25] He mounts the Capitol, where he offers a sacrifice of thanks. Now who, I ask you, has ever offered sacrifice for right desires, or for impulses in agreement with nature? We only thank the gods, it seems, for what we popularly suppose are the good things in life.

[26] ‚ÄòA man spoke with me today about accepting a priesthood of Augustus.31 I told him not to touch it. ‚ÄúYou will lay out a lot of money for little in return.‚Äù‚Äô

[27] ‚ÄòBut the clerk will add my name to public contracts.‚Äô

‚ÄòAre you planning to be there every time a contract is signed, so you can announce to the assembled, ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs my name he‚Äôs writing down there‚Äù? [28] Even if you can attend these signing ceremonies now, what will you do when you die?‚Äô

‚ÄòBut my name will survive me.‚Äô

‚ÄòCarve it in stone and it will survive you just as well. Outside Nicopolis, though, no one is going to remember you.‚Äô

[29] ‚ÄòBut I get to wear a crown of gold.‚Äô

‚ÄòIf you have your heart set on wearing crowns, why not make one out of roses ‚Äì you will look even more elegant in that.‚Äô

# 1.20 Concerning reason, and how it studies itself ‚Üë

[1] Every craft or faculty has a field with which it is primarily concerned. [2] When the faculty happens to be like what it studies, it naturally comes to study itself. When it is different, however, then it cannot. [3] To give an example: shoemaking works on leather, but the craft itself is quite distinct from leather; therefore, it does not study itself. [4] The art of grammar has to do with written speech, but is it written speech? No. Therefore, it cannot study itself.

[5] Now, for what purpose did nature arm us with reason? To make the correct use of impressions. And what is reason if not a collection of individual impressions? Hence, it naturally comes to turn its analysis on itself. [6] And what does the virtue of wisdom profess to investigate? Things good, bad and indifferent. And what is wisdom itself? Good. And ignorance? Bad. It is natural for wisdom too, then, to investigate itself, as well as its opposite.

[7] Therefore, the first and most important duty of the philosopher is to test impressions, choosing between them and only deploying those that have passed the test. [8] You know how, with money ‚Äì an area where we believe our interest to be much at stake ‚Äì we have developed the art of assaying, and considerable ingenuity has gone into developing a way to test if coins are counterfeit, involving our senses of sight, smell, hearing and touch. [9] The assayer will let the denarius drop and listen intently to its ring; and he is not satisfied to listen just once: after repeated listenings he practically acquires a musician‚Äôs subtle ear. [10] It is a measure of the effort we are prepared to expend to guard against deception when accuracy is at a premium.

[11] When it comes to our poor mind, however, we can‚Äôt be bothered; we are satisfied accepting any and all impressions, because here the loss we suffer is not obvious. [12] If you want to know just how little concerned you are about things good and bad, and how serious about things indifferent, compare your attitude to going blind with your attitude about being mentally in the dark. You will realize, I think, how inappropriate your values really are.

[13] ‚ÄòBut this calls for a lot of preparation, and hard study.‚Äô

So what? Do you think the greatest art can be acquired easily, and overnight? [14] It‚Äôs true that the principal doctrine of the philosophers is briefly stated; you have only to read Zeno to see my meaning. [15] How long does it take to say, ‚ÄòThe goal is to follow the gods,‚Äô and ‚ÄòThe essence of the good is the proper use of impressions‚Äô? [16] But, just because it is so brief, the formula prompts other questions: ‚ÄòWhat is God, and what is an impression? How does individual nature compare with the nature of the whole?‚Äô

Now the inquiry is beginning to drag out.

[17] Here we have Epicurus saying that goodness is none other than the flesh. But then there are lectures to sit through about man‚Äôs dominant principle ‚Äì that is to say, about what his substance and essence are ‚Äì and the explanation begins to grow long again. It‚Äôs not likely that the good of a snail lies in its shell, so is it likely that Epicurus is correct in identifying man‚Äôs good with his body?

[18] Take yourself, Epicurus. Even you have a faculty that is greater than the flesh ‚Äì the faculty which, having examined and thought through the evidence, concluded that flesh was the principal thing. [19] And why are you so fond of burning the midnight oil, working hard to produce so many books on our behalf? Obviously, it must matter to you that we be put in possession of the truth. But who are we, and, more to the point, who are we to you?

Well, now the explanation of Epicurus‚Äô views is growing lengthy too...

# 1.21 To people who want to be admired ‚Üë

[1] When someone is properly grounded in life, they shouldn‚Äôt have to look outside themselves for approval.

[2] ‚ÄòMy friend, what is it you want?‚Äô

‚ÄòI am satisfied if my desires and aversions agree with nature, if I exercise impulse and refusal as I was born to do, and if I practise purpose, design and assent the same way.‚Äô

‚ÄòSo why are you acting so stuck up?‚Äô

[3] ‚ÄòI want everyone I meet to admire me, to follow me around shouting, ‚ÄúWhat a great philosopher!‚Äù ‚Äô

[4] And who exactly are these people that you want to be admired by? Aren‚Äôt they the same people you are in the habit of calling crazy? And is this your life ambition, then ‚Äì to win the approval of lunatics?

# 1.22 On preconceptions ‚Üë

[1] Everyone has preconceptions. And one preconception does not contradict another. I mean, who of us does not assume that what is good is beneficial and choice, in all cases to be desired and pursued? Who of us does not assume that justice is fair and appropriate? So where does conflict come in? [2] In the application of preconceptions to particular cases. [3] One person, for instance, will say, ‚ÄòWell done, there‚Äôs a brave man,‚Äô while another says, ‚ÄòHe isn‚Äôt brave, he‚Äôs just deranged.‚Äô

This is how conflict originates, [4] and it is the source of difference among Jews, Syrians, Egyptians and Romans. They don‚Äôt dispute that what is holy should be preferred above everything else and in every case pursued; but they argue, for example, over whether it is holy or unholy to eat pork. [5] This is also the basis of the conflict between Agamemnon and Achilles. Call them up before us.‚Äò

.‚ÄòWhat do you say, Agamemnon? Shouldn‚Äôt we do what is right and proper?‚Äô

‚ÄòNaturally.‚Äô

[6] ‚ÄòWhat about you, Achilles? Wouldn‚Äôt you, of all people, say that we ought to do what is appropriate?‚Äô

‚ÄòOf course.‚Äô

‚ÄòAll right, then, apply your preconceptions.‚Äô

[7] It is just here that conflict starts. Because one says, ‚ÄòI shouldn‚Äôt have to return Chryseis to her father,‚Äô and the other says, ‚ÄòIndeed you‚Äôd better.‚Äô It‚Äôs obvious that one of them is misapplying his preconception of what is appropriate. [8] Then Agamemnon says, ‚ÄòFine, if I have to give Chryseis back, then I should get one of the other men‚Äôs prizes in return.‚Äô Achilles says, ‚ÄòIt‚Äôs not mine you intend to take, I hope.‚Äô ‚ÄòYours, that‚Äôs right.‚Äô ‚ÄòWhy should I be penalized?‚Äô ‚ÄòBecause it‚Äôs not right that I be the only one to go without.‚Äô And this is how conflicts originate.

[9] What does it mean to be getting an education? It means learning to apply natural preconceptions to particular cases as nature prescribes, and distinguishing what is in our power from what is not. [10] The operations of the will are in our power; not in our power are the body, the body‚Äôs parts, property, parents, siblings, children, country or friends.

[11] Where should we put the good, then ‚Äì to which of the two classes of things are we going to assign it? To the class of things in our power. [12] It follows that neither health nor fitness are good, nor are our children, parents or country.

‚ÄòThat view is not going to win you many friends or converts.‚Äô

[13] Well, let‚Äôs transfer the designation ‚Äògood‚Äô to health and other externals and see what happens. Suppose someone meets with misfortune and loses these ‚Äògoods‚Äô; can they still be happy? ‚ÄòImpossible.‚Äô You might ask, too, how they will remain on good terms with their neighbours, since we naturally incline to self- interest. [14] And if it is in my interest to own land, it is in my interest to rob them of it. If it is in my interest to own a coat, it is in my interest to pinch one from the baths. There you have the genesis of wars, factions and seditions.

[15] And how will I be able to stay right with God? Because if I‚Äôm harmed and meet with misfortune, I begin to doubt whether he is looking after me. And if he won‚Äôt help me, why should I give him my regard? I want nothing to do with a god who allows me to be in my present state. So I begin to hate him, [16] and wonder if the temples and statues we dedicate to him are not intended to placate a malignant force, like Fever. Honorary titles like ‚ÄòSaviour‚Äô, ‚ÄòRain- bringer‚Äô and ‚ÄòFruit-bringer‚Äô are no longer applicable to him ‚Äì and all this comes of identifying ‚Äòthe good‚Äô with externals.

[17] What should we do? That‚Äôs the topic of inquiry for the person who is truly out to philosophize and think deep. Now, if I am in the dark as to what is good and bad, I‚Äôm crazy. [18] But if I locate ‚Äòthe good‚Äô in the realm of the will I risk being ridiculed. Some grey old man rattling his gold rings and shaking his head at me will come along and say, ‚ÄòListen, my boy, philosophy‚Äôs all right up to a point, but don‚Äôt get carried away. This question is ridiculous. [19] Philosophers can teach you logic, but you know better than they do the right way to behave.‚Äô [20] ‚ÄòWell, if I know what to do, as you say, don‚Äôt make an objection if what I choose to do is philosophize.‚Äô I‚Äôm not going to try to engage this fatuous old fool in logic. But if I ignore him altogether, he‚Äôll explode in indignation. [21] So I have no choice but to say to him, ‚ÄòHumour me as you would someone in love. I can‚Äôt help myself, you see, I‚Äôm mad.‚Äô

# 1.23 Against Epicurus ‚Üë

[1] Even Epicurus realizes that we are social creatures by nature, but once he has identified our good with the shell,32 he cannot say anything inconsistent with that. [2] For he further insists -rightly ‚Äì that we must not respect or approve anything that does not share in the nature of what is good.

[3] So how is it that we are suspicious ‚Äì we who supposedly have no natural affection for our children?33 Why is it, Epicurus, that you dissuade the wise man from bringing up children? Are you afraid that he may become emotionally involved and unhappy? [4] And is that because you have been anxious on behalf of your house-slave Mouse?34

Well, what‚Äôs it to Epicurus, anyway, if his little Mouse comes crying to him?

[5] No, he realizes that, once a child is born, it is no longer in our power not to love or care for it. [6] Which is why he says that a man of sense will not take part in politics either; he knows the kinds of personal connections that politics involves. So what‚Äôs to keep us from living as if we were as unsocial as flies?

[7] But as if he didn‚Äôt know this, he has the gall to suggest that we should abandon our children. Even a sheep does not desert its own offspring, or a wolf; should a human desert his? [8] Would you have us be as foolish as sheep or as savage as wolves ‚Äì neither of which abandons its young? [9] Come on, whoever remembers your advice when they see their little child fallen and crying on the ground?

[10] Personally, I imagine that your own mother and father, even had they predicted that you were going to say such things, would not have exposed35 you.

# 1.24 How we should struggle with circumstance ‚Üë

[1] The true man is revealed in diffcult times. So when trouble comes, think of yourself as a wrestler whom God, like a trainer, has paired with a tough young buck. [2] For what purpose? To turn you into Olympic-class material. But this is going to take some sweat to accomplish. From my perspective, no one‚Äôs diffculties ever gave him a better test than yours, if you are prepared to make use of them the way a wrestler makes use of an opponent in peak condition.

[3] Now we are sending you to Rome as a spy. And we don‚Äôt want one who is easily frightened, or one who will turn back at the first sound of noise, or glimpse of shadow, announcing hysterically that the enemy is practically at the gates. [4] If you tell us on your return, ‚ÄòConditions are terrible in Rome, everywhere death, exile, poverty, informants ‚Äì everything a shambles. Fly, the enemy is upon us!‚Äô [5] ‚Äì we will respond by telling you in future to keep your forecasts to yourself. Our only mistake was in sending a spy like you in the first place.

[6] Diogenes36 went scouting before you did and came back with a very different report. Death, he said, was not evil because it was not dishonourable. Reputation was the empty noise of fools. [7] And he said other things that helped remove the element of fear from pain and poverty. In his manner of life he preferred the minimum of clothing to a purple gown, and the bare ground to a bed, however soft. [8] And as proof of such claims, he produced his assurance, his serenity, his freedom -as well as his tough, radiant physique.

[9] ‚ÄòThere is no enemy near by,‚Äô he said. ‚ÄòAll is peace and tranquillity.‚Äô

‚ÄòExplain, Diogenes.‚Äô

‚ÄòLook for yourself: am I wounded, disabled or in flight from any enemy force?‚Äô

[10] That‚Äôs the kind of spy we honour. You bring us back a report full of a lot of random noise. Go off and make a better search, this time without the trepidation.

[11] ‚ÄòWhat should I do then?‚Äô

What do you do when you leave a ship? Do you walk off with the rudder and oars? No; you leave with your own gear, your oil-flask and wallet. So just remember what belongs to you, and you won‚Äôt lay claim to what doesn‚Äôt.

[12] The emperor says to you, ‚ÄòRemove your broad hem.‚Äô

‚ÄòVery well, I‚Äôll wear the narrow hem.‚Äô

‚ÄòRemove that too.‚Äô

‚ÄòAll right, I‚Äôll wear the ordinary toga now.‚Äô37

‚ÄòTake your toga off.‚Äô

‚ÄòFine, I‚Äôll go naked.‚Äô

[13] ‚ÄòNow your very composure provokes me.‚Äô

‚ÄòTake my whole body, then.‚Äô

Is there any reason to fear someone to whom I stand ready to surrender my miserable corpse?

[14] But so-and-so will not leave his estate to me. Well? I forgot that none of it was mine. How then do we call it mine? As we call the bed in an inn mine. If the innkeeper dies and leaves you his bed, fine; but if he leaves it to someone else, then he will have it, and you will find a replacement. [15] And if you don‚Äôt, then you will have to sleep on the ground. Only rest easy there and snore away, because, remember, tragedies take place among the rich ‚Äì among kings, and potentates. No poor man swells a tragedy except as a member of the chorus. [16] Kings start off well enough: ‚ÄòDeck the palace halls.‚Äô But then around the third or fourth act, we get, ‚ÄòO Cithaeron, why did you receive me?‚Äô38 [17] Fool, where are your crowns, your diadem? Even your guards can‚Äôt help you now.

[18] So when you stand before one of those tyrants, just bear in mind that you are in the presence of a tragic figure ‚Äì and not the actor, either, but Oedipus himself.

[19] ‚ÄòBut he‚Äôs so lucky to be able to walk around with an entourage.‚Äô

Well, I too mingle with the masses and so am attended by an entourage. [20] The chief thing to remember is that the door is open.39 Don‚Äôt be a greater coward than children, who are ready to announce, ‚ÄòI won‚Äôt play any more.‚Äô Say, ‚ÄòI won‚Äôt play any more,‚Äô when you grow weary of the game, and be done with it. But if you stay, don‚Äôt carp.

# 1.25 More on the same theme ‚Üë

[1] If what we‚Äôve been saying is true and we aren‚Äôt being ridiculous, or merely pretending to believe that what is good or bad for us lies in the will and that we are indifferent to everything else ‚Äì then why do we continue to experience fear and anxiety? [2] No one has power over our principles, and what other peopledo control wedon‚Äôt care about. So what is your problem, still?

[3] ‚ÄòMy problem is that I want specific instructions on how to act in line with these principles.‚Äô

What other orders do you need than those Zeus has given you already? He has given you what is your own unrestricted and unrestrained; what is not yours he has made restricted and restrained. [4] What commandment, then, did you arrive with when he sent you here? ‚ÄòProtect what belongs to you at all costs; don‚Äôt desire what belongs to another.‚Äô Trustworthiness is your own, decency and a sense of shame; no one can take them from you or prevent you from using these qualities except yourself ‚Äì which you do the moment you begin to care about what isn‚Äôt yours, surrendering what is yours in the process.

[5] With such directions and commands from Zeus, what additional ones do you hope to get from me? Am I greater or more to be trusted? [6] Keep his commandments and you won‚Äôt need others. And as proof that he has delivered them to you, bring your preconceptions to bear. Bring the arguments of philosophers. Bring what you‚Äôve often heard, and often said yourself; what you‚Äôve read, and what you‚Äôve practised.

[7] Just how long should we apply these precepts that we have from God, before breaking up the game? [8] Just so long as the game remains a pleasure. At the Saturnalia a king of the revels is chosen by chance, because this is the convention.40 Then our ‚Äòking‚Äô hands out orders: ‚ÄòDrink up! You there, mix the wine! You, sir, give us a song! You, join the party; while you there ‚Äì get lost!‚Äô And we play along with him, so that the game will not be spoiled on our account. [9] But if the ‚Äòking‚Äô says, ‚ÄòImagine that you are unhappy,‚Äô and I demur, who is going to force me? [10] If, on the other hand, the programme calls for the re-enactment of Achilles‚Äô quarrel with Agamemnon, and the actor in the role of king says to me, ‚ÄòGo and get Briseis away from Achilles,‚Äô [11] I‚Äôll go. When he says, ‚ÄòReturn,‚Äô I‚Äôll return.

The way we handle hypothetical arguments can also serve as a model for our behaviour.41 ‚ÄòLet‚Äôs assume that it is night.‚Äô

‚ÄòFine.‚Äô

‚ÄòThen is it day?‚Äô

‚ÄòNo, because I‚Äôve accepted the hypothesis that it is night.‚Äô

[12] ‚ÄòLet‚Äôs assume, in the manner of a game or play, that you pretend to believe that it is night.‚Äô

‚ÄòOK.‚Äô

‚ÄòNow, believe that it really is night.‚Äô

[13] ‚ÄòThat does not follow from the hypothesis.‚Äô

The same rules apply in life: ‚ÄòLet‚Äôs assume you‚Äôve come upon hard times.‚Äô

‚ÄòGranted.‚Äô

‚ÄòThen you are unfortunate.‚Äô

‚ÄòYes.‚Äô

‚ÄòAnd suffering.‚Äô

‚ÄòYes.‚Äô

‚ÄòNow believe that what has happened to you is bad.‚Äô

‚ÄòThat does not follow from the hypothesis. Besides, there is another who won‚Äôt let me.‚Äô42

[14] How long should we submit to the rules of the game? As long as it serves my turn, and I find the part congenial. [15] Some dour, inflexible types will say, ‚ÄòI can‚Äôt eat at this man‚Äôs table if it means listening to his war stories again: ‚ÄúI told you, friend, how I scrambled up the hill; now we came under renewed bombardment...‚Äù‚Äô [16] But another person in the same situation might say, ‚ÄòThe meal is what matters; let him rattle on to his heart‚Äôs content.‚Äô [17] It is for you to arrange your priorities; but whatever you decide to do, don‚Äôt do it resentfully, as if you were being imposed on. And don‚Äôt believe your situation is genuinely bad ‚Äì no one can make you do that. [18] Is there smoke in the house? If it‚Äôs not suffocating, I will stay indoors; if it proves too much, I‚Äôll leave. Always remember ‚Äì the door is open.

[19] ‚ÄòDo not remain in Nicopolis,‚Äô they say to me; so I don‚Äôt remain there.

‚ÄòDon‚Äôt stay in Athens either.‚Äô

So I quit Athens.

‚ÄòNot Rome either.‚Äô

So I abandon Rome.

[20] ‚ÄòLive on Gyara.‚Äô43

But for me living on Gyara amounts to more smoke in my house than I can stand. So I depart to the one place no one can stop me from going, where everyone is made welcome. [21] And when I remove my last piece of clothing ‚Äì my skin ‚Äì then no one can lay a hold of me any longer. [22] Which is why Demetrius was emboldened to say to Nero, ‚ÄòYou threaten me with death, but nature threatens you.‚Äô

[23] If I cherish my body, I make a slave of myself, if I cherish my property, I make a slave of myself; [24] because I‚Äôve disclosed the means to make me captive. When a snake pulls back its head, right away I think, ‚ÄòHit it just there, on the part that it‚Äôs protecting.‚Äô In the same way you may be sure that whatever you are seen to protect, that will become your enemy‚Äôs focus of attack. [25] Keep this in mind, then there will be no one you will need to fear or flatter.

[26] ‚ÄòBut I want to sit in the senators‚Äô gallery.‚Äô

Look, the crowd is of your own creation, you‚Äôre treading on your own toes.

[27] ‚ÄòBut how else am I to get a clear view of the stage?‚Äô

If you don‚Äôt want to be crowded, don‚Äôt attend the theatre. What‚Äôs the difficulty? Or wait until the show is over, then seat and sun yourself at leisure in the senators‚Äô seats. [28] In general, remember that it is we who torment, we who make difficulties for ourselves ‚Äì that is, our opinions do. What, for instance, does it mean to be insulted? [29] Stand by a rock and insult it, and what have you accomplished? If someone responds to insult like a rock, what has the abuser gained with his invective? If, however, he has his victim‚Äôs weakness to exploit, then his efforts are worth his while.

[30] ‚ÄòStrip him.‚Äô What do you mean, ‚Äòhim‚Äô? Take his garment, you mean, and remove that. ‚ÄòI have insulted you.‚Äô ‚ÄòA lot of good may it do you.‚Äô [31] That is what Socrates practised, maintaining always the same even temper. But it seems that we would practise and study anything rather than how to remain free and unenslaved.

[32] ‚ÄòPhilosophers speak in paradoxes.‚Äô

And what of the other arts ‚Äì are they different? What is more paradoxical than cutting into a person‚Äôs eye to restore their vision? If someone suggested this procedure to a person ignorant of medicine, they would laugh in the practitioner‚Äôs face. [33] Little wonder, then, if many of the truths of philosophy also impress the masses as paradoxical.

# 1.26 What is the law of life? ‚Üë

[1] When a person was reading the hypothetical arguments, Epictetus said, ‚ÄòThis is a law concerning hypotheses, that we must accept what follows by way of conclusion. Even more cogent is the law of life that obliges us to act in accordance with nature. [2] If we mean to obey it in every area, on every occasion, clearly we must never allow what nature prescribes to escape us, and allow into our lives what runs contrary to nature.

[3] ‚ÄòSo philosophers start us out with logic, since it‚Äôs easier, reserving more problematic subjects for later. In the study of logic, there is nothing to distract us; whereas in practical matters our attention is constantly pulled in other directions. [4] Whoever insists on jumping right into practical matters risks making a fool of himself, since it‚Äôs not easy tackling harder subjects first. [5] And this is the right defence to use with parents upset with their children because they study philosophy: ‚ÄúHave it your way, father: my judgement is poor and I don‚Äôt know what I really should be doing. But if this can neither be learned nor taught, then don‚Äôt blame me for it. If it can, however, then either teach it to me yourself, or let me learn it from someone who professes to know; [6] since I hope you don‚Äôt suppose that if I‚Äôm doing the wrong thing it‚Äôs by choice. So what else could explain my error but ignorance? [7] And wouldn‚Äôt you rather I be cured of that? When did anger, however, ever teach someone to play music or pilot a ship? Do you imagine that your anger is going to help teach me the far more complex business of life?‚Äù

[8] ‚ÄòBut that line of argument assumes that such actually is your motive for studying philosophy; [9] anyone learning logic and attending philosophy lectures to make a show of erudition at a dinner party is satisfied just to win the respect of some senator seated beside him. [10] For that is where the important matters are, and treasures there are accounted trifles here. Consequently, it is hard to be master of one‚Äôs impressions in matters of ethics, where things that muddle the judgement are so many.

[11] ‚ÄòI know of one man who cried, clinging to Epaphroditus‚Äô knees, saying how miserable he was now that he was down to his last million. [12] And what did Epaphroditus do ‚Äì laugh at him as you are laughing now? No, he was appalled, and said, ‚ÄúDear man, how did you keep silent up till now? How have you endured it?‚Äù ‚Äô

[13] When Epictetus corrected the person reading the hypothetical arguments and the person who had set him the reading began ridiculing him, Epictetus said, ‚ÄòYou are laughing at yourself. You did not prepare the student properly or make sure he could understand these arguments. And still you use him as a reader. [14] Well then,‚Äô he said, ‚Äòif a mind doesn‚Äôt have the aptitude to follow and judge a conjunctive argument, why do we assume it‚Äôs capable of awarding praise, assigning blame or forming a judgement about things good and bad? If that person thinks ill of a person, will the other person care? Or if he admires someone, will that someone jump for joy ‚Äì when in such minor matters of logic he can‚Äôt make an inference or draw a correct conclusion?

[15] ‚ÄòThis, then, is the beginning of philosophy ‚Äì an awareness of one‚Äôs own mental fitness. Consciousness of its weakness will keep you from tackling difficult subjects. [16] As it is, though, some people strain at a pamphlet and still want to devour an entire treatise. Naturally they can‚Äôt digest it, and get heartily sick of the whole business. [17] They need to first figure out what their capacity is. In the realm of logic it is easy to refute someone ignorant of the subject. But in the affairs of life, no one offers himself to be examined, and whoever presumes to examine us we resent. [18] And yet Socrates used to say that the unexamined life was not worth living.‚Äô

# 1.27 In how many ways impressions arise, and what aids we should provide against them. ‚Üë

[1] Impressions come to us in four ways: things are and appear to be; or they are not, and do not appear to be; or they are, but do not appear to be; or they are not, and yet appear to be. [2] The duty of an educated man in all these cases is to judge correctly. And whatever disturbs our judgement, for that we need to find a solution. If the sophisms of the Pyrrhonists and the Academics44 are what trouble us, we must look for the antidote. [3] If it is the plausibility of things, causing some things to seem good that are not, let us seek a remedy there. If it is habit that troubles us, we must try to find a corrective for that.

[4] What aid can we find to combat habit? The opposed habit. [5] You hear people commonly saying things like, ‚ÄòHe died, the poor man‚Äô; ‚ÄòHis father perished, his mother too‚Äô; ‚ÄòHe was cut down in his prime, and in a foreign land.‚Äô [6] Lend your ear to different descriptions, distance yourself from statements such as these, check one habit with its opposite. Against sophistry one should have the practice and exercise of rational argument. Against specious impressions one should have clear preconceptions polished and ready to hand.

[7] When death appears an evil, we should have ready the fact that it is a duty to avoid evil things, whereas death is necessary and cannot be avoided. [8] I mean, where am I going to go to get away from it? Maybe I am not Sarpedon, the son of Zeus, so that I can say in the same grand manner, ‚ÄòI will go, and either win the prize for valour myself, or give another the opportunity to gain it.‚Äô45 The former may be beyond us, but at least the latter is within our reach.

[9] And where can I go to escape death in any case? Tell me the country, give me the name of the people who are safe from death, where I can get asylum; provide me with the magic charm. No, I cannot escape death, [10] but at least I can escape the fear of it ‚Äì or do I have to die moaning and groaning too?

Passions stem from frustrated desire; [11] so if I am able to shape conditions to suit my wishes, I will do so, but if not, I am ready to maul anyone who stands in my way. [12] People by nature cannot endure being deprived of the good or meeting up with evil. [13] And if, in the end, I can‚Äôt alter circumstances, or tear to pieces the person who prevents me, then I sit and bawl, abusing everyone I can think of, including Zeus and the other gods. For if they do not look after me, why should I care about them? [14] ‚ÄòBut you will be blasphemous.‚Äô Well, what punishment could they devise in that case that would make my situation worse? Remember that, as a rule, unless piety and self-interest can be reconciled, piety cannot be preserved. Don‚Äôt you find that convincing?

[15] Let the Pyrrhonist and Academic philosophers step forward to face us.

‚ÄòFor my part, I have no leisure for these controversies, nor can I simply side with convention.‚Äô46

[16] Now, if I had a dispute about property I would have called in a lawyer to help with my case. What will meet my needs in this instance? [17] The process of perception ‚Äì whether it involves the entire body or only a part ‚Äì this I may not be able to explain, because either alternative seems problematic to me. But that you and I are not the same person ‚Äì of that I‚Äôm quite certain. [18] I mean, when I want to swallow something, I never carry it to your mouth, but my own. And when I want bread, I don‚Äôt grab a broom, I head straight for the bread like an arrow to its target.‚àó [19] And you Sceptics, who dismiss the evidence of the senses ‚Äì do you act any differently? Which one of you ever went to the mill when you were in need of a bath?

[20] ‚ÄòWell, shouldn‚Äôt we be diligent defenders of convention, then, and watchful for any attack on it?‚Äô [21] Certainly ‚Äì provided we have the leisure for controversies of this kind. The person who is trembling, upset and inwardly broken in spirit needs to spend his time differently, though.

# 1.28 That we should not be angry with people; and what people account great and small ‚Üë

[1] The cause of our assenting to the truth of something is that it appears to be fact. [2] And it is impossible to assent to anything that does not appear to be fact. Why? It is the mind‚Äôs nature: it will assent to the truth, reject what is false and suspend judgement in doubtful cases.

[3] Here, I will prove it to you: feel, if you can, that it is night now. ‚ÄòImpossible.‚Äô Don‚Äôt feel that it is now day. ‚ÄòImpossible.‚Äô Feel, or don‚Äôt feel, that the number of stars is even. ‚ÄòImpossible.‚Äô [4] So when someone assents to a false proposition, be sure that they did not want to give their assent, since, as Plato says, ‚ÄòEvery soul is deprived of the truth against its will.‚Äô47 [5] They simply mistook for true something false.

Now, with respect to our actions, the case is analogous, only instead of true and false we react to impressions of right and wrong, good and bad, honest and dishonest. [6] And it is impossible to think that an action will do us good and not choose to do it.

[7] What about Medea, though ‚Äì she who says:

I know that the acts I intend are wrong
But anger is the master of my intentions.

That only amounts to saying that she thinks gratifying her anger by exacting revenge on her husband is preferable to keeping her children safe.48 [8] ‚ÄòYes, but she is in error.‚Äô Well, demonstrate to her clearly that she is in error and she will not act on her idea. As long as you don‚Äôt lay it out for her, though, she has nothing besides her own idea of right and wrong to guide her. [9] So don‚Äôt get angry at the poor woman for being confused about what‚Äôs most important, and accordingly mutating from human being to snake. Pity her instead. We take pity on the blind and lame, why don‚Äôt we pity people who are blind and lame in respect of what matters most? [10] Whoever keeps in mind that our actions are all determined by our impressions, which can either be right or wrong ‚Äì now, if the impression is correct, we are innocent, but if it is incorrect we pay for it ourselves, since it is impossible that someone else should be penalized for our error ‚Äì whoever keeps this in mind will not be angry or upset with anyone, won‚Äôt curse, blame, resent or malign anyone either.

[11] ‚ÄòSo in your view great tragedies are merely the result of this ‚Äì somebody‚Äôs ‚Äúimpression‚Äù?‚Äô

The result of that and that alone. [12] You take the Iliad: it‚Äôs nothing but people‚Äôs impressions and how they dealt with them. An impression made Paris rob Menelaus of his wife, and an impression got Helen to run away with him. [13] Now, if an impression had come to Menelaus that perhaps he was better off losing such a wife ‚Äì well, that would have meant the loss to us not just of the Iliad but of the Odyssey as well.

[14] ‚ÄòSo you‚Äôre saying that matters so great owe their origin to something so trivial?‚Äô

But what do you mean by ‚Äòmatters so great‚Äô? Wars, factions, the loss of many men, the razing of cities ‚Äì tell me what‚Äôs so ‚Äògreat‚Äô about all that? [15] What‚Äôs so great about slaughtering many sheep or cattle, or burning a lot of storks‚Äô or swallows‚Äô nests?

[16] ‚ÄòCan you really compare the two?‚Äô Why not? In the one case human bodies are lost, in the other case the dead happen to be farm animals. People‚Äôs houses burn, so do storks‚Äô nests. [17] What‚Äôs so earth-shaking or awful in that? Show me how a house, considered merely as shelter, is better than a stork‚Äôs nest. [18] ‚ÄòHow can a stork be compared with a human being?‚Äô Where the body is concerned, there is lots of similarity, only in man‚Äôs case his body inhabits houses composed of bricks and timber, while storks‚Äô nests are made of sticks and mud.

[19] ‚ÄòSo is there no distinction between a person and a stork?‚Äô

Of course there is, but not in regard to these externals.
