[20] Reflect and you‚Äôll realize that man excels in other respects: in taking cognisance of his own behaviour; in being sociable, trustworthy and honourable; in learning from his own mistakes; in brains. [21] What counts as good and bad for man can be found precisely in those respects in which he differs from the beasts. If his special qualities are kept safe behind stout walls, and he does not lose his honour, trustworthiness or intelligence, then the man is saved. But lose or take away any of these qualities and the man himself is lost.

[22] Everything significant depends on this. Did Paris‚Äô tragedy lie in the Greeks‚Äô attack on Troy, when his brothers began to be slaughtered? [23] No; no one is undone by the actions of others. That was the destruction of storks‚Äô nests. His tragedy lay in the loss of the man who was honest, trustworthy, decent and respectful of the laws of hospitality. [24] Wherein did Achilles‚Äô tragedy lie? The death of Patroclus? Not at all. It was that he gave in to anger, that he whined about losing a mere woman and lost sight of the fact that he was there not for romance but for war. [25] Those are the genuine human tragedies, the city‚Äôs siege and capture ‚Äì when right judgements are subverted; when thoughts are undermined.

[26] ‚ÄòWomen are driven into captivity, children are enslaved, and men are put to the sword. Is none of this bad?‚Äô

[27] What‚Äôs the basis for adding that description? Let me in on it too.

‚ÄòWhy don‚Äôt you explain instead how they are not evils?‚Äô

[28] Let‚Äôs turn to our standards, produce our preconceptions. I mean, this is what flabbergasts me. If there‚Äôs a question about weight, we don‚Äôt formulate a judgement at random; if it‚Äôs a matter of judging straight and crooked, we don‚Äôt make our decision based on whim. [29] If the truth of the case makes any difference to us at all, then none of us operates in the dark. [30] Yet when it comes to the first and foremost cause of good and bad conduct; when it‚Äôs a matter of doing well or ill, of failure or success ‚Äì only then do we proceed blindly and erratically, only then are we found to lack anything like a scale or measure. A sense impression appears and right away I react. [31] Am I better than Agamemnon and Achilles, insofar as they do and suffer such wrongs by following their impressions, while the impression does not satisfy me? [32] Is there any tragedy with a different source? What is the Atreus of Euripides? An impression. The Oedipus of Sophocles? An impression. The Phoenix? An impression. Hippolytus?49 An impression. [33] What kind of person, then, pays no attention to the matter of impressions, do you think? Well, what do we call people who accept every one indiscriminately?

‚ÄòMadmen.‚Äô

And do we act any differently?

# 1.29 On steadfastness ‚Üë

[1] The essence of good and evil consists in the condition of our character. [2] And externals are the means by which our character finds its particular good and evil. [3] It finds its good by not attaching value to the means. Correct judgements about externals make our character good, as perverse or distorted ones make it bad.

[4] God has fixed this law, and says, ‚ÄòIf you want something good, get it from yourself.‚Äô But you say, ‚ÄòNo, I‚Äôll get it from another.‚Äô I say, ‚ÄòNo ‚Äì get it from yourself.‚Äô [5] If a tyrant threatens me at court, I say, ‚ÄòWhat is he threatening?‚Äô If he says, ‚ÄòI will put you in chains,‚Äô I say, ‚ÄòHe is threatening my hands and feet.‚Äô [6] If he says, ‚ÄòI will behead you,‚Äô I say, ‚ÄòHe is threatening my neck.‚Äô If he says, ‚ÄòI will throw you into prison,‚Äô I say, ‚ÄòHe is threatening my entire body‚Äô; if he threatens exile, I say the same. [7] ‚ÄòWell, then, aren‚Äôt you threatened, even a little?‚Äô If I feel that these things are nothing to me, then no. [8] But if I fear for any of them, then, yes, it is I who am threatened.

Who is there left for me to fear, and over what has he control? Not what is in my power, because no one controls that except myself. As for what is not in my power, in that I take no interest.

[9] ‚ÄòSo do you philosophers teach contempt for rulers?‚Äô

Not at all. You don‚Äôt find any of us preaching defiance of them within their range of competence. [10] My body, my property, my standing in society, my friends ‚Äì they can have them all. And I challenge anyone to say that I encourage others to lay claim to those things.

[11] ‚ÄòYes,‚Äô the king says, ‚Äòbut I want control over your judgements too.‚Äô Who gave you that power? You cannot add the judgements of others to your conquests. [12] ‚ÄòI will win by way of fear.‚Äô You do not seem to realize that the mind is subject only to itself. It alone can control it, [13] which shows the force and justice of God‚Äôs edict: the strong shall always prevail over the weak. [14] ‚ÄòTen are stronger than one.‚Äô Yes, for what, though? For taking people captive, for killing or dragging them off, for taking away their property. For main force, yes, ten are better than one. [15] But one person with right judgements is superior to ten without. Numbers here are irrelevant. Put them in the balance, the person with correct ideas will outweigh all the others.

[16] ‚ÄòTo think of the indignities Socrates suffered at the hands of the Athenians!‚Äô Not ‚ÄòSocrates,‚Äô stupid, express yourself more carefully: ‚ÄòTo think that Socrates‚Äô poor body should have been seized and hauled off to prison by men stronger than it, that somebody gave his poor body hemlock, that it grew progressively colder until it was dead.‚Äô [17] What seems unjust, or outrageous, in this, or worth blaming the gods for? Didn‚Äôt Socrates have resources to compensate? [18] Where was the essence of the good for him ‚Äì and who is more to be heeded in that regard, him or you? ‚ÄòAnytus and Meletus can kill me, but they cannot harm me,‚Äô50 he says, and: ‚ÄòIf it pleases the gods, so be it.‚Äô

[19] Produce a person who can get the better of someone whose judgements are superior. You can‚Äôt, though, try as you might. This is God‚Äôs law and nature‚Äôs: ‚ÄòLet the best man win.‚Äô But ‚Äòbest‚Äô in his area of expertise. [20] One body is stronger than another body, many bodies are stronger than one; a thief has the advantage here over one who is not a thief. [21] This is how I came to lose my lamp:51 the thief was better than I am in staying awake. But he acquired the lamp at a price: he became a thief for its sake, for its sake, he lost his ability to be trusted, for a lamp he became a brute. And he imagined he came out ahead!

[22] Fine words, you may say ‚Äì but now I have been seized by the cloak and am being dragged downtown. Bystanders shout, ‚ÄòHey, philosopher, what good did your views do you after all? Look, you‚Äôre being hauled off to prison and soon will be beheaded.‚Äô [23] Tell me, what Introduction to Philosophy could I have read that would have saved me from being dragged away if a stronger man grabs me by the cloak; or could have kept me out of prison if I am assaulted by a gang of ten? [24] What philosophy has taught me, though, is to be indifferent to events beyond the will‚Äôs control. [25] Haven‚Äôt you profited in this respect too? So don‚Äôt look for help from philosophy except in areas where you have learned that help from it can be found. [26] As I sit in prison I can say, ‚ÄòWhoever laughs at me is deaf to the real meaning of words, can‚Äôt understand what they hear, and doesn‚Äôt even care to know what it is philosophers say or do. Let them be.‚Äô

[27] But the answer comes: ‚ÄòCome out of prison.‚Äô If you have no further need of me in prison, I‚Äôll come out; if you need me, I‚Äôll go back in again. [28] ‚ÄòHow long will you keep this up?‚Äô For as long as the mind chooses to be with the body. But when the mind no longer consents, then you can take my body, and farewell to it. [29] Only, we must not part with it rashly or irrationally, or on trivial pretext. Because, again, God does not wish it. He needs us, he needs the world that we help populate. If he sounds the signal for retreat, though, as he did for Socrates, we must obey the signal as if it came from our commander-in-chief.

[30] Well, should we try telling this to the ignorant mob? [31] What would be the point? It‚Äôs enough if we are convinced of it ourselves. When children come up to us clapping their hands and shouting, ‚ÄòToday is good Saturnalia,‚Äô do we say, ‚ÄòThe Saturnalia is not ‚Äúgood‚Äù?‚Äô Of course not, we clap our hands right along with them. [32] As for you, if you can‚Äôt change a person‚Äôs mind, realize that he is no more than a child ‚Äì and clap hands with him. And if you can‚Äôt bring yourself to do that, then just keep quiet.

[33] It is essential that we remember this, so that, when troubles arise, we will know that it‚Äôs time to exhibit what we‚Äôve learned. [34] A student fresh out of school who gets into diffculty is like someone practised in the solving of syllogisms; if anyone gives him an easy one, he says, ‚ÄòGive me a knotty one instead, I want a bit of practice.‚Äô In the same way, athletes don‚Äôt like to be paired with pushovers. [35] ‚ÄòHe can‚Äôt lift me,‚Äô one says, ‚Äòthis other guy is better built.‚Äô No, when the crisis comes, we groan and say, ‚ÄòI wanted to keep on learning.‚Äô Keep learning what? If you didn‚Äôt learn these things in order to demonstrate them in practice, what did you learn them for?

[36] I suppose there might be some who are sitting here losing patience and thinking, ‚ÄòWhy don‚Äôt I get to face the kind of challenge he did? I am growing old in a corner, when I could be winning a crown at Olympia! When will I be nominated for a similar trial?‚Äô

That is the attitude that all of you should adopt. [37] There are gladiators at Rome who get frustrated if they are not called out and matched with an opponent, all the while begging God and their own supervisors to be allowed to do battle one-on-one. None of you here shows anything like the same mettle. [38] Which is why I would like to escape to Rome to see my favourite wrestler in action, how he, at least, puts policy into practice.

[39] ‚ÄòThese are not the circumstances that I want.‚Äô Is it up to you to choose them? You have been given that particular body, these particular parents and brothers, this particular social position and place to live. You come to me hoping that I can somehow change these circumstances for you, not even conscious of the assets that are already yours that make it possible to cope with any situation you face. [40] ‚ÄòIt is yours to choose the exercise, mine to manage it well.‚Äô Right, but instead you say, ‚ÄòDon‚Äôt set me that kind of hypothetical argument, give me that one instead; don‚Äôt give me that compound proposition, give me that other one.‚Äô [41] The time is coming when actors of tragedy will identify with their masks, their high-heeled boots, and their long robe. Wake up, those are props representing your circumstances and situation. [42] Say something so that we‚Äôll know whether you are a tragic actor or the comic relief ‚Äì because of the costume you two have in common. [43] Does the tragic actor disappear, if you take away his boots and mask and bring him onstage a mere shadow of his former self ‚Äì or is he there still? If he has the right voice, he remains.

[44] So it is in life: take a governorship. ‚ÄòI take it and, when I take it, I show how a real philosopher acts.‚Äô [45] Take off your senator‚Äôs robe and put on beggar‚Äôs rags ‚Äì and let‚Äôs have a look at you then. ‚ÄòWell, so what? I still have the gift of a fine voice to show off.‚Äô [46] What role do you appear in now? As a witness called by God. [47] ‚ÄòStep forward, you, and bear witness for me; you earned the right to represent me as a witness. Is anything good or bad that is independent of your will? Do I do any man harm? Have I put each man‚Äôs advantage under the control of anyone except himself?‚Äô What witness do you bear God? [48] ‚ÄòI‚Äôm in difficulty, lord, and pitiable: no one cares about me, no one helps me; I‚Äôm the object of universal scorn.‚Äô [49] Is that the witness you are going to bear, making a mockery of God‚Äôs summons, when he honoured you and judged you worthy to be his public spokesman?

[50] But what if someone in authority pronounces you godless and atheistic?52 How are you affected? ‚ÄòI have been judged godless and atheistic‚Äô [51] Nothing more? ‚ÄòNothing.‚Äô If he had passed judgement on a conditional argument, and said, ‚ÄòThe proposition that ‚ÄúIf it is day, it is light‚Äù I declare to be false, what has happened to the conditional? Who is judged in this case? Who has been condemned ‚Äì the conditional, or the person who got it wrong?‚Äô

[52] Well, then, who is this man who is empowered to pass judgement on you? Does he know anything about what is religious or irreligious? Has he studied and learned about it? Where, and from whom? [53] You know, a musician has no consideration for anyone who mistakes the highest string on the lyre for the lowest; and anyone who affirms that, in a circle, lines that extend from the centre to the circumference can be unequal is not going to win the respect of mathematicians. [54] So ‚Äì a true philosopher is under no obligation to respect vulgar opinion as to what is religious or irreligious, what is just or unjust. What dishonour he brings on philosophers in general if he did! That‚Äôs not what you learned here.

[55] Wouldn‚Äôt you rather leave petty arguments about these subjects to do-nothings who sit in a corner and receive their little stipend, or get nothing and whine about it? Step forward and make use of what you‚Äôve learned. [56] It isn‚Äôt more logic chopping that is needed ‚Äì our Stoic texts are full of that. What we need now are people to apply their learning and bear witness to their learning in their actions. [57] Please, be the one to take on this character, I am tired in my teaching of invoking examples from the past, I want to be able to hold up an example from my own time.

[58] Consideration of these factors belongs to people who have the leisure for it; because man is an animal made for abstract thought. [59] But, for our honour‚Äôs sake, let‚Äôs not be seen studying them as if we were slaves who have run away. We should sit composed, without distraction, listening now to the tragic actor, now to the musician ‚Äì not act like escapees who are moved to praise the performers while continually glancing nervously around, and who come apart completely if their master‚Äôs name is dropped. [60] It is beneath the dignity of philosophers to study nature in this spirit. For what does ‚Äòmaster‚Äô mean? Man is not the master of another man, only death and life are, pleasure and pain.

So bring Caesar and not these other things before me and I am resolute. [61] But when he comes in thunder and lightning brandishing these things, and I show fear in response, in effect I have been brought face to face with my master, just like a runaway slave. [62] Even while I have a reprieve from these factors, my attention to the performance is no better than the slave‚Äôs. I wash, drink, sing, but all in a spirit of gloom and foreboding. [63] If, however, I liberate myself from my master ‚Äì which is to say, from the emotions that make my master frightening ‚Äì what troubles can I have? No man is my master any longer.

[64] Well, is it our duty to announce these truths to everyone? No, instead we need to make allowances for people without the benefit of education, and say to ourselves, ‚ÄòHe is telling me to do this because he imagines it‚Äôs good for himself as well; so I can‚Äôt blame him.‚Äô [65] Socrates himself forgave his jailer when he began to drink the hemlock, and the man broke down in tears, saying, ‚ÄòIt shows great generosity of spirit for him to grieve for us.‚Äô [66] Does he say to the jailer, ‚ÄòThat‚Äôs why we wouldn‚Äôt let the women in!‚Äô No, he only says that to his close friends ‚Äì to those who can assimilate it. The jailer, though, he indulges as he would a child.

# 1.30 How to prepare for trouble ‚Üë

[1] In the event that you are haled before someone wielding the reigns of power, remember that there is somebody else looking down from above, and you have to answer first to him. [2] So he examines you: ‚ÄòHow did you categorize exile, imprisonment, chains, death and disgrace, when you were in school?‚Äô

‚ÄòI said they were indifferent.‚Äô

[3] ‚ÄòAnd what do you call them now? They haven‚Äôt changed, I presume?‚Äô

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

‚ÄòWell, have you changed?‚Äô

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

‚ÄòThen define for me now what the ‚Äúindifferents‚Äù are.‚Äô

‚ÄòWhatever things we cannot control.‚Äô

‚ÄòTell me the upshot.‚Äô

‚ÄòThey are nothing to me.‚Äô

[4] ‚ÄòRemind me what you thought was good.‚Äô

‚ÄòThe will and the right use of impressions.‚Äô

‚ÄòAnd the goal of life is what?‚Äô

‚ÄòTo follow God.‚Äô

[5] ‚ÄòAnd do you stand by that now?‚Äô

‚ÄòI say it even now.‚Äô

‚ÄòGo, then, in confidence, holding fast to these convictions. You‚Äôll see what it‚Äôs like to be a young person with an education, alongside people who have none. [6] I promise that you will feel somewhat like this: ‚ÄúWhy do we serve such a long and difficult apprenticeship ‚Äì in preparation to face nonentities? [7] Is this what ‚Äòauthority‚Äô meant? Are the courtyards, the palace staff, the guards no more than this? Was this why I sat through so many lectures? It all amounts to nothing ‚Äì and I was expecting to be overwhelmed.‚Äù ‚Äô

# Book 2 ‚Üë

# 2.1 That confidence does not conflict with caution ‚Üë

[1] To some people, perhaps, what we philosophers say will appear impossible. But let us investigate, all the same, whether it‚Äôs true that in our daily lives we can act with both caution and confidence. [2] It seems impossible because the two are evidently opposites ‚Äì and opposites (supposedly) cannot coexist. But what most people consider strange I think can be explained on the following hypothesis: [3] if we are talking about using confidence and caution on the same objects, we might fairly be accused of trying to reconcile irreconcilables. But our claim does not involve anything so strange.

[4] We have often said, and shown, that the use of impressions represents for us the essence of good and evil, and that good and evil have to do with the will alone. And if that is true, [5] then nothing is impractical in the philosophers‚Äô advice to ‚ÄòBe confident in everything outside the will, and cautious in everything under the will‚Äôs control.‚Äô [6] For if evil is a matter of the will, then caution is needed there; and if everything beyond the will and not in our control is immaterial to us, then those things can be approached with confidence. [7] And so, you see, that‚Äôs how we can be cautious and confident at the same time ‚Äì and, in fact, confident owing to our caution. For, being on our guard against evils, we approach things whose nature is not evil in a spirit of assurance.

[8] Instead, however, we act like deer. When deer are frightened by the feathers, they seek safety in the hunters‚Äô nets.1 Confusing ruin with refuge, they come to an ill-timed death. [9] Similarly, fear afflicts us in matters outside the will‚Äôs control, while we act confidently and casually in matters dependent on the will as if they were of no importance. [10] To be deceived or rash, to act shamelessly or with unbridled lust ‚Äì none of this matters to us as long as we have success in affairs outside the will. Death, exile, pain and ill repute ‚Äì there you will find the impulse to tremble and run away.

[11] So, you would expect when error involves the things of greatest importance, our natural confidence is perverted into rashness, thoughtlessness, recklessness and shamelessness. At the same time, all fear and agitation, we exchange our natural caution and reserve for meekness and timidity. [12] Transfer caution to the will and the functions of the will, and the mere wish will bring with it the power of avoidance. But if we direct it at what is outside us and is none of our responsibility, wanting instead to avoid what‚Äôs in the control of others, we are necessarily going to meet with fear, upset and confusion. [13] Death and pain are not frightening, it‚Äôs the fear of pain and death we need to fear. Which is why we praise the poet who wrote, ‚ÄòDeath is not fearful, but dying like a coward is.‚Äô

.‚Äô[14] So be confident about death, and caution yourself against the fear of it ‚Äì just the opposite, in other words, of what we are doing now. Now we shrink from death, whereas our views about death hardly concern us, we hardly give them a thought, and are completely apathetic. [15] Socrates used to call such fears ‚Äòhobgoblins‚Äô,2 and rightly so; just as masks scare and frighten children since they haven‚Äôt seen them before, we react to events in much the same way and for much the same reason.

[16] What is a child? Ignorance and inexperience. But with respect to what it knows, a child is every bit our equal. [17] What is death? A scary mask. Take it off ‚Äì see, it doesn‚Äôt bite. Eventually, body and soul will havetoseparate, justasthey existed separately before we were born. So why be upset if it happens now? If it isn‚Äôt now, it‚Äôs later. [18] And why now, if that happens to be the case? To accommodate the world‚Äôs cycle; because the world needs things to come into being now, things to come into being later ‚Äì and it needs things whose time is now complete.

[19] Pain too is just a scary mask: look under it and you will see. The body sometimes suffers, but relief is never far behind. And if that isn‚Äôt good enough for you, the door stands open; otherwise put up with it. [20] The door needs to stay open whatever the circumstances, with the result that our problems disappear.3 [21] The fruit of these doctrines is the best and most beautiful, as it ought to be for individuals who are truly educated: freedom from trouble, freedom from fear ‚Äì freedom in general. [22] The masses are wrong to say that only freeborn men are entitled to an education; believe the philosophers instead, who say that only educated people are entitled to be called free. [23] I will explain. What else is freedom but the power to live our life the way we want?

‚ÄòNothing.‚Äô

Do you want to live life doing wrong?

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

Therefore, no one doing wrong is free. [24] Do you want to live your life in fear, grief and anxiety?

‚ÄòOf course not.‚Äô

So no one in a state of constant fear is free either. By the same token, whoever has gained relief from grief, fear and anxiety has gained freedom. [25] What confidence, then, can we have in our own dear legislators when they say that only freeborn people are entitled to an education, when the philosophers contend that only people already educated can be considered free? God will not allow it, you see.

[26] ‚ÄòWhat about a master who performs the ceremony of manumitting a slave? Is nothing accomplished by that?‚Äô

Certainly it is ‚Äì the master has performed the ceremony; and, on top of that, he has paid a five per cent tax to the state.

[27] ‚ÄòBut his slave ‚Äì hasn‚Äôt he come by his freedom in the process?‚Äô

No more than he has instantly come by peace of mind. I mean, consider your own case: you have slaves, and the power to free them. [28] But what master, I wonder, do you yourself serve? Money? Women? Boys? The emperor or one of his subordinates? It has to be one of them, or you wouldn‚Äôt fret about such things.

[29] This is why I so often repeat to you the need to think about them and have these thoughts ready to hand, namely, the knowledge of what you should treat with confidence, and what you should treat with caution ‚Äì that you can treat with confidence whatever lies outside the will, but must treat with caution what lies within.

[30] But you say, ‚ÄòDidn‚Äôt I read to you, and didn‚Äôt you take note of my performance?‚Äô

[31] I noticed your clever phrases, yes ‚Äì and you can have them. Show me instead how you practise desire and aversion to get what you want and avoid what you do not want. As for those treatises of yours, if you have any sense, you will go and burn them.

[32] ‚ÄòBut wasn‚Äôt Socrates a writer, and a prolific one at that?‚Äô4 Yes, but for what purpose? Since there wasn‚Äôt always someone available whose ideas he could examine or who could examine Socrates‚Äô own in turn, sometimes he would test and examine himself, forever subjecting to scrutiny one assumption or another. [33] That‚Äôs the writing of a real philosopher; whereas pretty phrases in dialogue form he leaves to others ‚Äì aesthetes and idlers who lie about and have no patience with logical reasoning because they‚Äôre too stupid.

[34] Even now, if the opportunity presents itself, I know you will go off to read and make public those compositions, and you‚Äôll pride yourself on being fluent in the dialogue form. [35] Don‚Äôt do it, man. What I would rather hear from you is, ‚ÄòLook how I don‚Äôt fail in my desires, or have experiences I don‚Äôt want. I‚Äôll prove it to you in the case of death, I‚Äôll prove it to you in the case of physical pain, in the case of prison, of condemnation, and ill repute.‚Äô That‚Äôs the real test of a youth fit to finish school. [36] Forget about that other stuff, don‚Äôt let people hear you giving public recitations; and even if someone praises you, restrain yourself, be content to look like a nobody or know-nothing.

[37] Show them this, though, that you know how not to fail in your desires or experience what you don‚Äôt desire. [38] Let others practise lawsuits, solve logical puzzles or syllogisms. Your duty is to prepare for death and imprisonment, torture and exile ‚Äì [39] and all such evils ‚Äì with confidence, because you have faith in the one who has called on you to face them, having judged you worthy of the role. When you take on the role, you will show the superiority of reason and the mind over forces unconnected with the will. [40] Then that paradox will no longer seem so paradoxical or absurd ‚Äì that we should be confident and cautious at the same time: confident in relation to things outside the will, cautious about things within.

# 2.2 On tranquillity ‚Üë

[1] If you are headed to court, consider carefully what it is you want to keep and in what area you want to win. [2] If you want to keep your character in line with nature, you have every hope of success, all the means you need, and not a worry in the world. [3] Because if you want to keep what is yours by right and is by nature free ‚Äì and these are the only things you want ‚Äì you have nothing to worry about. No one else controls them or can take them away from you. [4] If you want to be a man of honour and a man of your word, who is going to stop you? You say you don‚Äôt want to be obstructed or forced to do something against your will ‚Äì well, who is going to force you to desire things that you don‚Äôt approve, or dislike something against your better judgement?

[5] Ah, but they will threaten you with punishments that overawe you. But how can they make you think of those sufferings as something you are obligated to avoid? [6] As long as desire and aversion are under your control, there is nothing more to worry about. [7] There is your opening statement, your exposition, your proof ‚Äì and there lies success, the last word, and acquittal. [8] Which is why Socrates, when he was told to prepare himself for trial, said, ‚ÄòHaven‚Äôt I been preparing for it my whole life?‚Äô [9] Preparing for it how? ‚ÄòI‚Äôve tended to my own affairs, and never done anything in violation of the law, either publicly or privately.‚Äô

[10] If, however, you want to keep hold of externals ‚Äì your body, belongings and reputation ‚Äì then my advice to you is that your preparations better begin early and will have to be long. You will need to research the character of the judge, of course, and make a study of your opponent too. [11] If grovelling is called for, then be prepared to grovel ‚Äì to weep and holler too. [12] Whenever externals are more important to you than your own integrity, then be prepared to serve them the remainder of your life. Don‚Äôt hedge and agree to be their slave, then change your mind later; [13] commit to one or the other position at once and without reserve. Choose to be either free or a slave, enlightened or a fool, a thoroughbred or a nag. Either resign yourself to a life of abuse till you die, or escape it immediately. For God‚Äôs sake, don‚Äôt put up with years of abuse, and then change your mind! [14] This humiliation can be avoided before it begins: just decide now what you think is truly good and bad.

[15] I mean, do you think that, if Socrates had any concern for what others could do to him, he would have stood before the court and said, ‚ÄòAnytus and Meletus can kill me, but cannot harm me‚Äô? [16] Do you think he was fool enough not to see that this approach was going to lead to a very different result?5 So why not make remarks that are more provocative?

[17] It‚Äôs like my friend Heraclitus,6 who had a lawsuit about a piece of property on Rhodes. After proving to the jury that he had right on his side, he came to his concluding remarks and said, ‚ÄòBut I am not going to plead with you, and I don‚Äôt care what you decide. You are more on trial here than I am.‚Äô With the result that he sabotaged his case. [18] And why was it necessary? Don‚Äôt plead ‚Äì but don‚Äôt go on to say, ‚ÄòI won‚Äôt plead‚Äô ‚Äì not unless there is a point in provoking the jurors deliberately, as in Socrates‚Äô case. [19] As for you, if you‚Äôre planning some such parting shot, why bother to speak at all, why even show up at court? [20] Because if you want to be crucified, just wait, and the cross will come. But if reason demands that you appear and do your best to be persuasive, you must do what is entailed ‚Äì while safeguarding what is yours.

[21] Thus, it is stupid to say, ‚ÄòTell me what to do!‚Äô7 What should I tell you? It would be better to say, ‚ÄòMake my mind adaptable to any circumstance.‚Äô [22] Saying ‚ÄòTell me what to do‚Äô is like an illiterate saying, ‚ÄòTell me what to write whenever I‚Äôm presented with a name.‚Äô [23] If I say ‚ÄòJohn‚Äô and then someone else comes along and gives him ‚ÄòJane‚Äô instead of ‚ÄòJohn‚Äô to write, what is going to happen? How is he going to write it? [24] If you have learned your letters, though, you are ready for anything anyone dictates. If you are not prepared, I don‚Äôt know what I should tell you to do. Because there may be events that call for you to act differently ‚Äì and what will you do or say then? [25] So hold on to this general principle8 and you won‚Äôt need specific advice. If you hanker after externals you are going to be twirled round and round at the will of your master. [26] ‚ÄòWho‚Äôs my ‚Äúmaster‚Äù?‚Äô Whoever controls what you desire or dislike.

# 2.3 Addressed to people who recommend others to philosophers ‚Üë

[1] It was a shrewd reply that Diogenes9 gave the person who asked him for a letter of reference: ‚ÄòThe person whose favour you seek will know that you‚Äôre human as soon as he sees you; as to whether you‚Äôre a good or a bad one ‚Äì well, either he‚Äôs a competent judge of character or he isn‚Äôt. If he is, he can decide that on his own too, and if he‚Äôs not, a thousand letters from me aren‚Äôt going to make him one. [2] A coin might as well ask for a recommendation to get someone to declare it genuine. If that someone is trained in distinguishing authentic coins from counterfeit, it will speak for itself.‚Äô

[3] And so it is in life. We need standards such as exist in the case of silver, so that we can make a similar claim as the assayer‚Äôs: ‚ÄòBring me any coin at all, I will tell you whether or not it‚Äôs genuine.‚Äô [4] Instead, it is in analysing syllogisms that we like to be able to say, ‚ÄòBring me any you like, and I can judge whether it is or is not analytic.‚Äô10 Why? Because we know how to analyse arguments, and have the skill a person needs to evaluate competent logicians. [5] But in life what do I do? What today I say is good tomorrow I will swear is bad. And the reason is that, compared to what I know about syllogisms, my knowledge and experience of life fall far behind.

# 2.4 Addressed to someone who had been caught cheating on his wife ‚Üë

[1] Epictetus was saying that, as human beings, we are born to be faithful to one another, and that whoever denies this denies their humanity. Just then, a well-known scholar entered the room ‚Äì one who had been caught in adultery while in Rome. [2] So Epictetus continued, ‚ÄòBut what are we really doing when we throw away our innate faithfulness, to intrigue with our neighbour‚Äôs wife? We are ruining and destroying ‚Äì well, what? How about the man of trust, principle and piety that once was? [3] And is that all? Aren‚Äôt we also ruining the idea of neighbourliness, friendship and community? What position are we putting ourselves in? How am I supposed to deal with you now? As a neighbour? A friend? Some friend! A fellow citizen? But how can a fellow citizen like you be trusted?

[4] ‚ÄòLook, if you were a bowl so leaky that you were good for nothing any more, you would be tossed in the rubbish dump, and no one would take the trouble to pick you out. [5] What are we going to do with a human who can‚Äôt fill the most basic human role? Fine, you can‚Äôt function as a friend; how would you do as a slave? Who would have any confidence in you, though, even in that capacity? So how would you like to be tossed in the rubbish too, like a leaky bowl ‚Äì like dung?

[6] ‚ÄòThen you are heard to say, ‚ÄúNo one gives me any respect as a man of letters.‚Äù That‚Äôs because you‚Äôre a vile and worthless human being! It‚Äôs as if wasps were to protest that they get no respect; everyone runs away from them, some people will even swat them, given the chance. [7] Your prick is such as to infect everyone you sting with heartache and aggravation. What would you have us do with you? There isn‚Äôt any place you will fit.

[8] ‚ÄòHold it, though ‚Äì doesn‚Äôt nature intend women to be shared?11 I grant it ‚Äì but in the way a roast is shared among dinner guests. Very well, after each guest has received his share, if you feel like it, why not grab your neighbour‚Äôs portion too? Steal it when he‚Äôs not looking, or just stretch out your hand and grab all you can manage to put away. If you can‚Äôt actually snatch the meat, at least wipe your hands on it, and lick the grease off your fingers. What fine company you will make -just like Socrates‚Äô fellow diners in the Symposium.‚Äô12

[9] ‚ÄòWell, what about the theatre ‚Äì it‚Äôs open to all citizens, is it not?‚Äô

‚ÄòSure, so when everyone has taken their seats, just come along and throw somebody out of theirs. [10] That, you may say, is how women are common property too.

‚ÄòLook, try this instead: when the judge, like the host at a private party, has assigned them, be satisfied to claim your own share, don‚Äôt be a glutton and steal someone else‚Äôs. ‚ÄúBut I‚Äôm a scholar who understands Archedemus!‚Äù [11] You can understand Archedemus and still be an adulterer and a cheater, a wolf or an ape rather than a human being; what‚Äôs to stop you?‚Äô

# 2.5 How confidence and carefulness are compatible ‚Üë

[1] Material things per se are indifferent, but the use we make of them is not indifferent. [2] The question, then, is how to strike a balance between a calm and composed attitude on the one hand, and a conscientious outlook that is neither slack nor careless on the other.13 Model yourself on card players. [3] The chips don‚Äôt matter, and the cards don‚Äôt matter; how can I know what the deal will be? But making careful and skilful use of the deal ‚Äì that‚Äôs where my responsibility begins.14 [4] So in life our first job is this, to divide and distinguish things into two categories: externals I cannot control, but the choices I make with regard to them I do control. Where will I find good and bad? [5] In me, in my choices. Don‚Äôt ever speak of ‚Äògood‚Äô or ‚Äòbad‚Äô, ‚Äòadvantage‚Äô or ‚Äòharm‚Äô, and so on, of anything that is not your responsibility.

[6] ‚ÄòWell, does that mean that we shouldn‚Äôt care how we use them?‚Äô

Not at all. In fact, it is morally wrong not to care, and contrary to our nature. [7] Be careful how you use them, because it‚Äôs not unimportant ‚Äì but at the same time be calm and composed, because things in themselves don‚Äôt matter. [8] Where it does matter, no one can compel me or stand in my way. And where I can be stopped or compelled, well, getting those things is not in my control ‚Äì and not good or bad in any case. But the way I use them is good or bad, and depends on me.

[9] It isn‚Äôt easy to combine and reconcile the two ‚Äì the carefulness of a person devoted to externals and the dignity of one who‚Äôs detached ‚Äì but it‚Äôs not impossible. Otherwise happiness would be impossible.15 [10] It‚Äôs something like going on an ocean voyage. What can I do? Pick the captain, the boat, the date, and the best time to sail. [11] But then a storm hits. Well, it‚Äôs no longer my business; I have done everything I could. It‚Äôs somebody else‚Äôs problem now ‚Äì namely the captain‚Äôs. [12] But then the boat actually begins to sink. What are my options? I do the only thing I am in a position to do, drown ‚Äì but fearlessly, without bawling or crying out to God, because I know that what is born must also die. [13] I am not Father Time; I‚Äôm a human being, a part of the whole, like an hour in a day. Like the hour I must abide my time, and like the hour, pass. [14] What difference does it make whether I go by drowning or disease? I have to go somehow.

[15] You will find that skilled ballplayers do the same thing. It‚Äôs not the ball they value, it‚Äôs how well they throw and catch it that counts as good or bad. [16] That is where the grace and skill lie, the speed and expertise ‚Äì speed such that I can‚Äôt catch one of their throws even if I spread out my coat to do it, and skill such that, if I throw the ball, however badly, one of them is bound to catch it.

[17] If we are afraid to throw the ball, or nervous about catching it, then the fun is lost; and how can we preserve our composure when we are uncertain about what next to do? ‚ÄòThrow it,‚Äô someone says, ‚ÄòDon‚Äôt throw it,‚Äô another says, ‚ÄòThrow it already!‚Äô says someone else. It turns into a shouting instead of a sporting match.16

[18] Socrates, you might say, knew how to play ball. In his case, the arena was the courtroom. ‚ÄòTell me, Anytus,‚Äô he said, ‚Äòhow can you say I don‚Äôt believe in God? We are agreed, are we not, that there are minor gods and heroes ‚Äì the children of gods or the mixed issue of gods and men?‚Äô [19] Anytus conceded the point. ‚ÄòWell, if someone acknowledged that there were animals of mixed parentage ‚Äì half breeds such as mules ‚Äì don‚Äôt you think they would also have to believe in the existence of horses or asses ‚Äì the creatures that produced them?‚Äô17 [20] It‚Äôs just as if the man were playing ball. Only the ball in his case was life, imprisonment, exile or execution ‚Äì with the prospect of losing his wife, and having his children reduced to the status of orphans. Those were the stakes of the game, and still he played, and handled the ball with aplomb.

[21] That‚Äôs what we need: the star athlete‚Äôs concentration, together with his coolness, as if it were just another ball we were playing with too. To be sure, external things of whatever kind require skill in their use, but we must not grow attached to them; whatever they are, they should only serve for us to show how skilled we are in our handling of them.

It‚Äôs like weaving: the weaver does not make the wool, he makes the best use of whatever wool he‚Äôs given. [22] God gives you food and property, and can take them back ‚Äì your body too. Work with the material you are given. [23] If you come through all right, most people you meet will congratulate you on surviving. A shrewd judge, however, will praise you and share in your pleasure only if he sees that you acted honourably in the case; not, however, if he sees that your success was owed to anything dishonest. When happiness is come by fairly, others are happy for us too.

[24] ‚ÄòThen how are some externals said to be in accordance with nature, others contrary to nature?‚Äô18 That only applies to us considered separately. I agree that for my foot it is in accordance with its nature to be clean; but considered as a foot and not separately, it is right and proper for it to tramp through mud, step on needles ‚Äì there may even be a time when it will have to be amputated for the sake of the body as a whole. It wouldn‚Äôt be a foot otherwise.

We have to assume that a similar distinction applies to us personally. [25] What are you? A human being. If you think of yourself as a unit apart, then it is in accordance with your nature to live to old age, to be rich, and be healthy. But if your view of yourself involves being part of a whole, then, for the sake of the whole, circumstances may make it right for you to be sick, go on a dangerous journey, endure poverty, even die before your time. Don‚Äôt complain; [26] just as it would not be a foot, don‚Äôt you realize that in isolation you would not be a human being? Because what is a human being? Part of a community ‚Äì the community of gods and men, primarily, and secondarily that of the city we happen to inhabit, which is only a microcosm of the universe in toto.

[27] ‚ÄòAnd that‚Äôs why now I‚Äôm being put on trial?‚Äô And why someone else falls sick, why another undertakes a voyage, why someone else dies, and still another is convicted. In this body, this universe, this community, it is inevitable that each of us faces some such event. [28] Your job, then, is to appear before the court, say what you have to say and make the best of the situation. [29] Then the judge declares you guilty. ‚ÄòI wish you well, judge. I did my part, you can decide if you did yours.‚Äô Because the judge runs a risk too, don‚Äôt forget.

# 2.6 On ‚Äòindifference‚Äô19 ‚Üë

[1] The conjunctive argument20 is indifferent, but how you handle it is not indifferent; it is tantamount to knowledge, opinion, or ignorance. In the same way, life is indifferent, but the use we make of it is not indifferent. [2] So when you hear that even life and the like21 are indifferent, don‚Äôt become apathetic; and by the same token, when you‚Äôre advised to care about them, don‚Äôt become superficial and conceive a passion for externals.

[3] It is good to be clear about the level of your talent and training. That way, when unfamiliar topics arise, you will know enough to keep still, and not be put out if there are students more advanced than you. [4] You will show your own superiority in logic; and if others are disconcerted over that, mollify them by saying, ‚ÄòWell, I had a good teacher.‚Äô [5] The same applies to subjects that require some practical training; don‚Äôt pretend you have a particular skill if you don‚Äôt yet; yield to whoever has the requisite experience; and for your own part take satisfaction in an awareness that your persistence is helping you become expert in the subject yourself.

[6] ‚ÄòGo pay so-and-so your respects.‚Äô

‚ÄòI call on him ‚Äì but not on my knees.‚Äô

‚ÄòAnd you were not let in.‚Äô

‚ÄòWell, you see, I don‚Äôt know how to break through windows. When I find the door shut, either I have to go through the window or leave.‚Äô

[7] ‚ÄòSo, talk to him.‚Äô

‚ÄòOK, I talk to him.‚Äô

‚ÄòHow?‚Äô

‚ÄòAs an equal.‚Äô

[8] But you did not get what you wanted ‚Äì because of course that was up to him, not you. So don‚Äôt take responsibility for it. Always remember what is yours, and what belongs to other people, and you won‚Äôt have trouble. [9] Apropos, Chrysippus said: ‚ÄòAs long as the future is uncertain to me I always hold to those things which are better adapted to obtaining the things in accordance with nature; for God himself has made me disposed to select them. [10] But if I knew that my destiny at present was to fall ill, I would even wish for it. My foot, too, if it had intelligence, would volunteer to get muddy.‚Äô

[11] Look, isn‚Äôt wheat grown for the express purpose of turning brown, and doesn‚Äôt it turn brown in preparation for being harvested? It is not grown for its own sake. [12] If it could talk, I suppose it would beg never to be harvested? Come, that‚Äôs actually a curse we put on people‚Äôs crop, that it not be brought to harvest. [13] Know that for humans, too, it‚Äôs no less of a curse not to die ‚Äì the same as ‚ÄòPlease, God, don‚Äôt let their wheat ripen, don‚Äôt let it be brought in.‚Äô [14] But because we‚Äôre the only animals who not only die but are conscious of it even while it happens, we are beset by anxiety. The reason can only be that we do not know who we are, and have not studied what it means to be a human being ‚Äì the way horse trainers, for instance, learn the ways of horses.

[15] Look at Chrysantas, though: just as he was poised to stab an enemy combatant he heard the trumpet sound retreat and froze; that‚Äôs how much he set the captain‚Äôs will above his personal welfare.22 [16] Yet even when fate calls, not one of us is prepared to obey. We suffer what we suffer not willingly, but with sobs of protest, and call it all ‚Äòcircumstances‚Äô. [17] What are circumstances? If by that you mean your situation, everything is ‚Äòcircumstances‚Äô. But if you mean ‚Äòproblems‚Äô, where‚Äôs the problem in something that was born, dying? [18] Death could come by way of a knife, torture, the sea, a piece of masonry, a despot ‚Äì why do you care? ‚ÄòAll roads to Hades are of equal length.‚Äô23 [19] Well, to tell you the truth, death by despot is less protracted. No despot ever took six months to cut a throat, whereas a fatal illness often lasts a year. ‚ÄòCircumstances‚Äô, ‚Äòtroubles‚Äô ‚Äì this is all a lot of noise and a clamour of meaningless names.

[20] You say, ‚ÄòI risk execution by the emperor.‚Äô

I face no less a danger here in Nicopolis, where earthquakes are a common occurrence. As to you, aren‚Äôt you risking your life every time you cross the Adriatic?

[21] ‚ÄòBut even one‚Äôs opinions can get one into trouble here in Rome.‚Äô

Do you mean your own? No one can force you to hold an opinion against your will. And if you are referring to other people‚Äôs, how can their wrong opinions pose any danger to you?

[22] ‚ÄòI also face the danger of exile.‚Äô

What is exile? Being somewhere other than Rome?

‚ÄòExactly. My God, what if I‚Äôm sent to Gyara?‚Äô Well, if that‚Äôs tolerable for you, you will go; if not, you have the choice of another destination, the place even the person who sent you to Gyara is headed, whether they like it or not.

[23] So why make such a big deal of going to trial? It isn‚Äôt worth all the preparation. A young man with any talent might well say, ‚ÄòI wasted my time listening to so many lectures, writing so many compositions, and sitting for so long next to an old man who didn‚Äôt amount to much himself.‚Äô

[24] There is only one thing you need to remember, the rule that distinguishes what is yours from what isn‚Äôt. Don‚Äôt ever lay claim to anything belonging to others. [25] Court and prison are two places, one high, the other low. Your character, however, can be kept the same in either place ‚Äì if you decide it should. [26] We will rival Socrates when we can spend our time in prison composing hymns. [27] But considering our attitude up to now, I wonder if in prison we could even stand someone else offering to read us his own compositions.

‚ÄòDon‚Äôt bother me; don‚Äôt you realize the problems I‚Äôve got?‚àó You think I can listen to poetry in my position?‚Äô

‚ÄòWhy, what is it?‚Äô

‚ÄòI‚Äôm sentenced to death!‚Äô

‚ÄòAnd the rest of us aren‚Äôt?‚Äô

# 2.8 What is the substance of the good? ‚Üë

[1] God is helpful. Whatever is good is also helpful. It is reasonable to suppose, then, that the divine nature and the nature of the good will correspond. [2] So what is the divine nature? Is it flesh? Be serious. Do we associate it with real estate and status? Hardly. It is mind, intelligence and correct reason.

[3] So look no further than there for the substance of the good. Of course, you won‚Äôt find it in plants and animals. In man, however, it consists in just those qualities that distinguish him from other animals. [4] Since plants do not even have the power of perception, ‚Äògood‚Äô and ‚Äòevil‚Äô are not applicable to them. Evidently, ‚Äògood‚Äô and ‚Äòbad‚Äô presume the power of using impressions. [5] But is that enough? If it is, then you must speak of ‚Äògood‚Äô in connection with animals besides man, as well as ‚Äòhappy‚Äô and ‚Äòunhappy‚Äô ‚Äì [6] but there is a very good reason that you don‚Äôt. Because, however well they may use impressions, animals lack the ability to reflect on them. Nor should they have it, since they were bornto serve, not command. [7] Is the donkey in a position of command? It was created because man needed an animal with a strong back able to carry big loads; the creature had to be able to walk, as well. Which is why it was endowed with the power of using impressions ‚Äì otherwise it couldn‚Äôt walk. And that is about the extent of its endowments. [8] After all, if the donkey had also acquired the ability to reflect on its use of impressions, it would quite rightly refuse to obey us and serve our needs. It would, in fact, be mankind‚Äôs equal and peer.

[9] So if it is absent from plants and animals, it‚Äôs only logical that you should look for the nature of the good where you ordinarily apply the word. [10] True, they are God‚Äôs creatures too, but not creatures placed in a position of authority, not parts of God. [11] You, on the other hand, are a creature placed in charge, and a particle of God himself; there is a bit of God within you.

[12] Why don‚Äôt you know of this relation, and of your origins? When you eat, bear in mind who it is exactly you are feeding. When you have sex, reflect who you are during the act. In conversation, exercise, discourse ‚Äì do you remember that it is God you are feeding, God you are exercising? You carry God around with you and don‚Äôt know it, poor fool. [13] Don‚Äôt imagine I am talking about some external deity made of silver or gold. You carry the living God inside you and are blind to the fact that you desecrate him with your dirty words and dirty thoughts ‚Äì [14] none of which you would dare repeat if there were even a mere statue of a god near by. God himself is there within, seeing and overhearing everything you do and say ‚Äì and do you care? You pariah, you have no sense of your own heritage.

[15] What are we anxious about when we graduate a young man from school and out into the real world? That he will make mistakes, eat poorly, have sexual affairs, humiliate himself and go around in rags, or else affect the latest fashions. And why? [16] Because he is ignorant of his personal god and does not realize who goes with him when he leaves school and his former friends. Yet we indulge him when he writes to us to say, ‚ÄòI wish you here with me.‚Äô [17] You have God there with you ‚Äì who else do you need? Whoever you invite to come and visit ‚Äì would they tell you any different?

[18] Suppose you were one of Phidias‚Äô statues ‚Äì his Zeus or his Athena.24 You would certainly have a sense of who you were then, and of who brought you into being. And if you had a brain, you would make an effort to avoid doing anything that would bring shame on either your creator or yourself, such as being caught in an embarrassing position by the spectators come to see you. [19] But Zeus has created you; shouldn‚Äôt that make you even more careful about the impression you make? What is Phidias compared to Zeus? How, for that matter, can their creations be compared? [20] What other work of art comes ready equipped with the very powers the artist displayed in making it? Do marble statues? No, nor do bronze, gold or ivory ones. The Athena of Phidias, once its arm was raised to support the statue of Victory, has maintained that pose for the duration of its long existence. Zeus‚Äô works, on the other hand, are living, breathing creatures, with the power of perception and judgement.

[21] He made you, and you mock him. Why? He not only made you; of all his creatures, you alone were given the power of self- determination. [22] You not only ignore that, you bring shame on the faith he placed in you. You would not have been so negligent in caring for an orphan if God had put one in your way. [23] He has entrusted you to yourself, saying, ‚ÄòI had no one more dependable than you; just see that he keeps the qualities he was born with: integrity, honour, dignity, patience, calmness and poise.‚Äô But you can‚Äôt even do that.

[24] However, there will be people who say, ‚ÄòWhy is this person so serious and self-important?‚Äô If it seems like pretension, it‚Äôs only because I don‚Äôt have complete confidence in the principles that I‚Äôve learned and espouse. I still fear for my own frailty. [25] But grant me the confidence, and I will show you the right look and bearing; then you will see the finished statue all bright and gleaming. [26] And don‚Äôt expect a study in smugness. After all, the Zeus at Olympia does not project an air of hauteur. He looks at us right in the eye ‚Äì just the way one ought to look when on the point of saying: ‚ÄòMy word is true and irrevocable.‚Äô25

[27] That is the sort of person you‚Äôll find I am: trustworthy, honourable, noble and poised. [28] Not, to be sure, immune to death, age or disease, like God, but still prepared to die and face illness with a godlike dignity. [29] That much is mine to do, even if I cannot accomplish the rest.

In short, I will show you that I have the strength ‚Äì of a philosopher. ‚ÄòAnd what strength would that be?‚Äô A will that never fails to get what it wants, a faculty of aversion that always avoids what it dislikes, proper impulse, careful purpose and disciplined assent. That‚Äôs the human specimen you should prepare yourselves to see.

# 2.10 Social roles as a guide to conduct ‚Üë

[1] Who are you? In the first place, a human being, which is to say, a being possessed of no greater faculty than free choice, with all your other faculties subordinate to it, choice itself being unconfined and independent. [2] Next, consider the gift of reason: it sets you apart from wild animals; it sets you apart from sheep. [3] By virtue of these two faculties you are a member of the universe with full citizen rights; you were born not to serve but to govern, because you understand the divine order and its pattern.

[4] Now, what does the title ‚Äòcitizen‚Äô mean? In this role, a person never acts in his own interest or thinks of himself alone, but, like a hand or foot that had sense and realized its place in the natural order, all its actions and desires aim at nothing except contributing to the common good. [5] Therefore, philosophers rightly say, ‚ÄòIf a good person knew that sickness, death or disability lay in his future, he would actually invite them, because he realizes that this is part of the universal plan and that the universe has precedence over a constituent, and the city over any one citizen. [6] But since we don‚Äôt know the future, we‚Äôre justified in sticking to things that are preferable by nature, because this, after all, is our instinct from birth.‚Äô

[7] Next, remember that you are somebody‚Äôs son. What does this social role mean? It means regarding everything of yours as belonging to your father as well, always letting him have his way, never trying to hurt him with your words or actions, or griping about him behind his back. Defer to him at every opportunity, and in the same spirit cooperate with him as best you can.

[8] Next, know that you are a brother. This role also calls for deference, respect and civility. Never get into family fights over material things; give them up willingly, and your moral standing will increase in proportion. [9] Make a gift of your box seat in the theatre, or a bit of food, if that‚Äôs at stake, and see the gratitude you get in return ‚Äì how much greater it is than the sacrifice.

[10] Finally, reflect on the other social roles you play. If you are a council member, consider what a council member should do. If you are young, what does being young mean, if you are old, what does age imply, [11] if you are a father, what does fatherhood entail? Each of our titles, when reflected upon, suggests the acts appropriate to it.

[12] If you go off and yell at your brother, my reaction is to say, ‚ÄòYou‚Äôve forgotten who you are and what you stand for.‚Äô [13] I mean, if you were a metalworker who fumbled with his tools, you would have lost touch with the metalworker you once were. If you forget what it means to be a brother and become your brother‚Äôs enemy, don‚Äôt think you‚Äôve made a trivial exchange. [14] If you are transformed from a decent, social human being into some mean, snarling, dangerous beast, is there no loss involved? Or do you have to lose money before you feel penalized? Is losing money the only loss that counts with us?

[15] If you lost the capacity to read, or play music, you would think it was a disaster, but you think nothing of losing the capacity to be honest, decent and civilized. [16] Yet those other misfortunes come from some outside cause, while these are your own fault. Moreover, it is neither honourable to have those other abilities nor dishonourable to lose them, whereas it is dishonourable to lose these capacities and a misfortune for which we have only ourselves to blame. [17] A catamite is deprived of his manhood, his seducer is no longer the man he was either, and is compromised in a hundred other ways besides. [18] An adulterer does away with a just, decent and honourable human being ‚Äì the good neighbour and citizen he might have been. A sorehead incurs one kind of loss, a coward another ‚Äì [19] but no one is bad without loss or penalty of some kind.

Now, if you look for their penalty in terms of money, you might find them all safe and scot-free; they could even be helped and rewarded for their offence if they gain by it financially. [20] But, if money is your only standard, then consider that, by your lights, someone who loses their nose does not suffer any harm.

‚ÄòYes they do, they‚Äôre maimed physically.‚Äô

[21] But what if they are deprived just of the sense of smell ‚Äì in other words, isn‚Äôt there an associated psychic faculty, which is good to have and a misfortune to lose?

[22] ‚ÄòWhat do you mean by ‚Äúpsychic faculty‚Äù?‚Äô

Aren‚Äôt we born with a sense of fairness?

‚ÄòWe are.‚Äô

If you destroy it, is there no harm, is nothing sacrificed, don‚Äôt we lose something dear? [23] Don‚Äôt we have an innate sense of honour, a sense of benevolence, a sense of kindness and compassion? Well, if someone willingly parts with these sensibilities, do you suppose they go unpunished and unhurt?

[24] ‚ÄòWell, does that mean that if someone wrongs me I shouldn‚Äôt hurt them in return?‚Äô First of all, look at what wrongdoing is and remember what you have heard about it from philosophers.26 [25] Because if ‚Äògood‚Äô as well as ‚Äòbad‚Äô really relate to our choices, then consider whether your position does not amount to saying something like, [26] ‚ÄòWell, since that guy hurt himself with the injustice he did me, shouldn‚Äôt I wrong him in order to hurt myself in retaliation?‚Äô

[27] So why don‚Äôt we actually picture it to ourselves this way? Instead, we see injury only where physical or financial loss is incurred, whereas if the loss stems from our own choices, then we don‚Äôt suspect any harm has been done. [28] After all, we don‚Äôt get a headache after an error in judgement or an act of injustice; we don‚Äôt get eye trouble or stomach ache, we don‚Äôt lose property. [29] And for us those are the only things that matter. As to whether our character will remain loyal and honest, or become false and depraved, we don‚Äôt care about that in the least ‚Äì except insofar as it comes up for examination in school; [30] the result being that our debating skills improve at the cost of our character.

# 2.11 Starting philosophy ‚Üë

[1] People who come to philosophy the right way ‚Äì by the front door, as it were ‚Äì begin by acknowledging their own faults and limitations in areas of most urgency. [2] The isosceles triangle and the half-tone or quarter-tone scale ‚Äì we have no knowledge pertaining to these subjects when we come into the world. We have to learn about them later through specialized training; with the result that no one untrained in these subjects pretends to know about them.

[3] The situation is otherwise in the case of our ideas about good and bad, right and wrong, appropriate and inappropriate, happiness, duty, and obligation. We are born with an innate understanding of what these words mean. [4] So everyone uses them, which is to say, everyone tries to apply the related preconceptions to specific things. [5] ‚ÄòHer action was good, it was her duty, it was not her duty, she was fortunate, she was not fortunate, she is honest, she is dishonest...‚Äô You hear such phrases all the time, because no one is in any doubt about how to use the words. No one feels they have to wait to be told their meaning, the way we once had to learn our letters or be taught how words are pronounced. [6] The reason is that we come into the world knowing some things that nature, you might say, has taught us already; and, building on this knowledge, we come to form our opinions.

[7] ‚ÄòWell, whatever you may say, I know good from bad, and have an idea of the good.‚Äô

You have one, I allow.

‚ÄòAnd I put it into practice.‚Äô

You use it in specific instances, yes.

‚ÄòAnd I use it correctly.‚Äô

[8] Well, that‚Äôs the crux, because this is where opinions become an issue. Starting with the ideas we take for granted, we get into arguments whenever we apply them incorrectly. [9] If, along with the innate ideas, we came into the world with knowledge of how they should be applied, we would be perfect wise men from the moment we were born.

[10] Now, you think that you do possess this extra knowledge of how to apply preconceptions in particular instances. How did you come by it?

‚ÄòIt‚Äôs my opinion.‚Äô

But someone else has a different opinion, and also believes that he is the one applying his preconceptions correctly. Am I right?

‚ÄòI guess so.‚Äô

[11] And if you clash over the application of preconceptions, you can‚Äôt both be using them correctly.

‚ÄòAgreed.‚Äô

[12] Well, can you come up with a better aid to their application than your opinion? I mean, a lunatic acts in accordance with his own opinion of what is good; but in his case can it function as an adequate guide?

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

So let‚Äôs move beyond opinion; is there nothing better?

[13] Here you have philosophy‚Äôs starting point: we find that people cannot agree among themselves, and we go in search of the source of their disagreement. In time, we come to scorn and dismiss simple opinion, and look for a way to determine if an opinion is right or wrong. At last, we focus on finding a standard that we can invoke, just as the scale was invented to measure weights, and the carpenter‚Äôs rule devised to distinguish straight from crooked. [14] That is the beginning of philosophy.

Are everyone‚Äôs opinions correct? How can they be, when some conflict?

[15] ‚ÄòAll right, not all are correct. But ours are.‚Äô

Well, why ours instead of the Syrians‚Äô or Egyptians‚Äô ‚Äì why mine rather than those of any person picked at random?

‚ÄòNo reason.‚Äô

Therefore, the fact that someone holds this or that opinion will not suffice to make it true, any more than we are inclined to trust a person‚Äôs word in dealing with weights and measures. In both cases, we have developed an objective standard instead. [16] So is there no standard for our case beyond opinion? Is it only humanity‚Äôs most important values that are going to remain vague and subjective? [17] There must be one. So let‚Äôs hunt for it; and once we‚Äôve found it, let‚Äôs commit to never making a single move without reference to it. [18] I conceive this discovery as the antidote to the madness that results from exclusive reliance on opinion as the criterion of truth. And from then on, starting with the familiar preconceptions, clearly defined, we will proceed to apply them to particular objects and events in a methodical manner.

[19] Name me a subject for discussion now.

‚ÄòPleasure.‚Äô

[20] All right, put it to the test, lay it on the scales. For something to be good, it must be something we can rely on and trust.

‚ÄòI agree.‚Äô

Well, can we trust anything that comes and goes?

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

[21] And is pleasure constant?

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

Then take it out of the scale and banish it from the realm of goods.

[22] But if you‚Äôre none too sharp and missed it the first time, let‚Äôs try a different test. Something good should be a source of pride, correct?

‚ÄòYes.‚Äô

And can one really take pride in a momentary pleasure? Please don‚Äôt say yes, or I will think you barely deserve to be regarded on the same level as draft animals.

[23] That is the way things are weighed and disagreements settled ‚Äì when standards are established. [24] Philosophy aims to test and set such standards. [25] And the wise man is advised to make use of their findings right away.

# 2.12 On the art of argumentation ‚Üë

[1] The philosophers of our school have told us precisely what we need to learn in order to know how to practise logical argument. But when it comes to practising it correctly in a book ii particular situation ‚Äì there we have no experience at all. [2] Just give us a random person to engage in dialogue and we won‚Äôt know what to do with him. After asking the person a few questions, if we don‚Äôt get the kinds of answer we expect, we throw up our hands and resort to ridicule or verbal abuse, saying, ‚ÄòHe‚Äôs not a philosopher, it just isn‚Äôt possible to engage him in dialogue.‚Äô

[3] Well, when a guide meets up with someone who is lost, ordinarily his reaction is to direct him on the right path, not mock or malign him, then turn on his heel and walk away. [4] As for you, lead someone to the truth and you will find that he can follow.27 But as long as you don‚Äôt point it out to him, don‚Äôt make fun of him; be aware of what you need to work on instead.

[5] Consider how Socrates behaved. He would compel whomever he spoke with to voice their views, and one interlocutor was enough. Which is why he could say, ‚ÄòEveryone else can go hang, I am only interested in what the person I‚Äôm talking to has to say. No one‚Äôs vote counts with me except that of my partner in dialogue.‚Äô28 [6] Socrates would lay out the implications of their views so incisively that, regardless of who they were, they would all admit an inconsistency and back off from it:

[7] ‚ÄòIs a man racked by envy happy?‚Äô

‚ÄòNot at all, they‚Äôre miserable.‚Äô

‚ÄòMiserable over something bad? But whoever heard of envy for something bad?‚Äô

[8] Thus he made him say that envy is pain provoked by something good.

‚ÄòI mean, would someone be envious over something he cared nothing for?‚Äô

‚ÄòObviously not.‚Äô29

[9] That is what Socrates would do: he would quit only after he had fleshed out an idea and explored its implications. He wouldn‚Äôt just say, ‚ÄòDefine envy for me,‚Äô then, when his discourses interlocutor had ventured on a definition, say, ‚ÄòWrong: your definiens is not extensionally equivalent to the definiendum‚Äô ‚Äì [10] technical terms which are incomprehensible and off ‚Äì putting for the layman, and which we can‚Äôt resist using for that very reason. [11] As for using language that would enable even a non-philosopher, depending on his view, to answer with a simple yes or no ‚Äì well, we don‚Äôt know how to engage anyone on that level. [12] And what happens is that we realize that we can‚Äôt do it and give up the attempt ‚Äì those of us with any discretion, anyway. [13] Most people are impulsive, however, and, having committed to the thing, they persist, just making more confusion for themselves and others until it all ends in mutual recrimination.

[14] Now that is the first thing Socrates was known for ‚Äì never turning dialogue into dispute, never introducing rudeness or invective, although he would put up with the insults of others in order to avoid a fight. [15] And if you want to know how effective he was, read Xenophon‚Äôs Symposium; you will see how many fights he is credited there with resolving. [16] Among the poets, too, one of the highest forms of compliment is conveyed in the line:

He could cut short a quarrel, however great, with his diplomacy.30

[17] But nowadays engaging in logical dialogue is just not a very safe business, in Rome especially. Because of course you can‚Äôt do it off in a corner somewhere. You may find yourself approaching a senator, someone with money, and asking him: ‚ÄòDo you know, sir, to whom you have entrusted the care of your horses?‚Äô

[18] ‚ÄòI do.‚Äô

‚ÄòIs it some random person with no knowledge of horses?‚Äô

‚ÄòOf course not.‚Äô

‚ÄòWhat about your money and clothes?‚Äô

‚ÄòI don‚Äôt hand them over to just anyone either.‚Äô

[19] ‚ÄòAnd as to your body, you‚Äôve already found someone to entrust with its care?‚Äô

‚ÄòNaturally.‚Äô

‚ÄòAn expert, obviously, in either exercise or medicine?‚Äô

‚ÄòYes.‚Äô

[20] ‚ÄòAre these the things you value most, or have you got something better than them all?‚Äô

‚ÄòAnd what would that be?‚Äô

‚ÄòThe faculty that uses all of them, and assigns each their place and value.‚Äô

‚ÄòYou mean the soul?‚Äô

[21] ‚ÄòGood guess; that‚Äôs precisely what I mean.‚Äô

‚ÄòAbsolutely, I think that is far and away a more precious possession than the others you mentioned.‚Äô

[22] ‚ÄòSo, then, tell me the steps you‚Äôve taken to care for the soul. As intelligent as you are, and as politically prominent, surely you would not casually look on and allow the most prized of your possessions to be neglected and go to ruin.‚Äô

‚ÄòNo, of course not.‚Äô

[23] ‚ÄòWell, if you have looked after it personally, did you learn how from another person, or discover the means yourself?‚Äô

[24] At this point you run the risk of him saying, ‚ÄòWhat business is that of yours, sir? What are you to me?‚Äô Pester him further, and he is liable to punch you in the nose. [25] I myself was once keen for this sort of discourse, until I met with just such a reception.

# 2.13 On nerves ‚Üë

[1] Whenever I see a person suffering from nervousness, I think, well, what can he expect? If he had not set his sights on things outside man‚Äôs control, his nervousness would end at once. [2] Take a lyre player: he‚Äôs relaxed when he performs alone, but put him in front of an audience, and it‚Äôs a different story, no matter how beautiful his voice or how well he plays the instrument. Why? Because he not only wants to perform well, he wants to be well received ‚Äì and the latter lies outside his control.

[3] He is confident as far as his knowledge of music is concerned ‚Äì the views of the public carry no weight with him there. His anxiety stems from lack of knowledge and lack of practice in other areas. Which are what? [4] He doesn‚Äôt know what an audience is, or what approval from an audience amounts to. Although he knows well enough how to play every note on the guitar, from the lowest to the highest, the approval of the public ‚Äì what it means and what real significance it has ‚Äì this he does not know and has made no effort to learn. [5] Necessarily, then, he is going to get nervous and grow pale. Now, I won‚Äôt go so far as to say that he‚Äôs not a true musician if I see that he suffers from stage fright. But I can say one thing ‚Äì several things, in fact.

[6] I can start by calling him a stranger and say, ‚ÄòThis person has no idea where he‚Äôs living, and for all his time in residence here still doesn‚Äôt know the laws of the country or its customs. He does not know what is permitted and what is not. Furthermore, he has never taken the trouble to call on a lawyer who will tell him, and explain how things operate here. [7] He won‚Äôt sign a contract without knowing how to draft one properly, or hiring somebody who does. He isn‚Äôt casual about signing for loans or offering guarantees. But when it comes to desire, aversion, impulse, plans and projects, he applies himself to all of these without benefit of legal counsel. [8] How do I know? He wants what he cannot have, and does not want what he can‚Äôt refuse ‚Äì and isn‚Äôt even aware of it. He doesn‚Äôt know the difference between his own possessions and others‚Äô. Because, if he did, he would never be thwarted or disappointed.

Or nervous.

Just think: [9] we aren‚Äôt filled with fear except by things that are bad; and not by them, either, as long as it is in our power to avoid them. [10] So, if externals are neither good nor bad, while everything within the sphere of choice is in our power and cannot be taken away by anyone, or imposed on us without our compliance ‚Äì then what‚Äôs left to be nervous about? [11] We agonize over our body, our money, or what the emperor is going to decree ‚Äì never about anything inside us.

I mean, do we worry whether we are going to make an error in judgement? No, because it is under our control. Or having book ii an unnatural urge? No again. [12] So if you see someone pale with nerves, be like a doctor who diagnoses liver trouble based on a patient‚Äôs yellow skin. Say, ‚ÄòThis man‚Äôs desire and aversion are unhealthy, they aren‚Äôt functioning properly, they‚Äôre infected. [13] Because nothing else can account for his change in colour, his shivering, his chattering teeth, and ‚Äúthis constant fretting and shifting from foot to foot‚Äù.‚Äô31

[14] All of which explains why Zeno was not nervous about his meeting with Antigonus.32 What Zeno valued Antigonus had no power over, and as a philosopher he cared nothing for the things that the king did command. [15] It was Antigonus who was anxious before their meeting. Naturally ‚Äì he wanted to make a good impression, which was beyond his control. Zeno, for his part, had no wish to please the king; no expert needs validation from an amateur. [16] So what do I need your approval for? You don‚Äôt know the measure of a man, you haven‚Äôt studied to learn what a good or a bad person is, and how each one gets that way. No wonder you‚Äôre not a good person yourself.

[17] ‚ÄòHow do you make that out?‚Äô

It is not in a good person‚Äôs nature to grieve, complain or whine; they don‚Äôt go pale, tremble and say, ‚ÄòWhat kind of hearing or reception will he give me?‚Äô [18] Idiot, that‚Äôs his concern ‚Äì don‚Äôt concern yourself with other people‚Äôs business. It‚Äôs his problem if he receives you badly.

‚ÄòTrue.‚Äô

And you cannot suffer for another person‚Äôs fault. So don‚Äôt worry about the behaviour of others.

[19] ‚ÄòAll right, but I worry about how I will talk to him.‚Äô

Can‚Äôt you talk to him any way you like?

‚ÄòI‚Äôm afraid that I may say something gauche.‚Äô

[20] Look, when you are about to spell the name ‚ÄòDion‚Äô, are you afraid that you will slip up?

‚ÄòNo.‚Äô

And why not? It‚Äôs because you have practice in writing the name.

‚ÄòTrue.‚Äô

And you would have the same confidence reading it.

‚ÄòYes.‚Äô

The reason is that any discipline brings with it a measure of strength and confodence in the corresponding arts. [21] Now, you have practice speaking. What else did they teach you at school?

‚ÄòSyllogisms and changing arguments.‚Äô33

But why, if not to be accomplished in conversation? And by accomplished I mean refined, assured, intelligent, not easily flustered or refuted ‚Äì and fearless, on top of all that.

‚ÄòAgreed.‚Äô

[22] Well, then, you are in the position of a soldier on horseback who is about to face a mere foot soldier, on ground that you have gone over and he has not. And still you‚Äôre nervous?

‚ÄòBut he can literally kill me!‚Äô

[23] Well, then, speak the truth, you sorry specimen, don‚Äôt put on airs and call yourself a philosopher. Face up to who your betters are. As long as you have this attachment to the body, be ready to submit to anyone or anything of superior physical force.

[24] As for speaking, Socrates must have practised the art, look at his answer to the Thirty Tyrants, his defence before the jury, his conversations in jail.34 Diogenes too had practised how to speak, witness the free and easy way he talked to Alexander, Philip, the pirates and the person to whom the pirates sold him as a slave.35 [26] As for you, go back to your work and don‚Äôt ever leave it. Settle back in your alcove, think up new syllogisms, and share them with your friends. [27] You are plainly not cut out for the role of public leader.

# 2.14 To Naso36 ‚Üë

[1] A visitor from Rome, together with his son, was present at one of his lectures when Epictetus said, ‚ÄòThis is the way I teach‚Äô ‚Äì then abruptly stopped talking. [2] The man pleaded with him to go on, but Epictetus said: The learning process is boring to anyone completely new to, and unfamiliar with, a skill. [3] Now, the skill‚Äôs finished product leaves no doubt as to its utility, most are even pleasing or attractive. [4] It is not exciting, for instance, to follow the progress of a shoemaker in his art, but shoes are not only useful, they are usually aesthetically pleasing to a degree. [5] Or the training of a carpenter ‚Äì it is very tedious for people who are not carpenters to watch, but the finished cabinetry justifies the effort. [6] Music makes my point most obviously: attend a music lesson, and you will think it involves the most monotonous training of all. But the results please and entertain everyone.

[7] In our school, we picture the philosopher‚Äôs goal more or less as follows: bring the will in line with events, so that nothing happens contrary to our wishes and, conversely, nothing fails to happen that we want to happen. [8] Pursue it, and the reward is that neither desire nor aversion will fail in their aims; and we will fill all our roles in society ‚Äì as son, father, brother, citizen, man, woman, neighbour, fellow voyager, ruler or ruled ‚Äì without conflict, fear or rancour.

[9] That is how we picture the philosopher‚Äôs goal. The next step is finding how to make it reality. [10] Becoming a carpenter or pilot, we realize, requires some formal training. Is it unreasonable to suppose that it will take more than just the desire to be good or bad ‚Äì that the student of philosophy will also have to learn a few things of his own? So we look for some guidance. [11] Philosophers say that the first thing to learn is that God exists, that he governs the world, and that we cannot keep our actions secret, that even our thoughts and inclinations are known to him. [12] The next thing to learn about is the divine nature, because we will have to imitate the gods if we intend to obey them and win their favour. [13] If the divine nature is trustworthy, then we should be trustworthy; if it is free, then we should be free; likewise if it is benevolent and forgiving. All our thoughts and behaviour should be shaped on the divine model.

[14] So where to begin? If you are prepared for it, I would say that you need to begin by understanding the meaning of words.

‚ÄòAre you implying that at present I don‚Äôt?‚Äô

I am.

[15] ‚ÄòThen how come I use them?‚Äô
