  39. the big one and the small: The big city is the cosmic community of god and man; cf. II 5, 26. ‚Ü©
  40. the water of Dirce: The fountain of Dirce was located in Thebes. ‚Ü©
  41. Ah, for Nero‚Äôs baths, and the water of Marcia: A parody of Euripides‚Äô Phoenissae 368: ‚ÄòThe gymnasia in which I was reared and the water of Dirce‚Äô (Polyneices speaking). The Marcian aqueduct brought water to the city of Rome. ‚Ü©
  42. Diogenes: This is Diogenes the Cynic. ‚Ü©
  43. Whoever can exit the party... until death: In other words, he would sooner die (‚Äòleave the party‚Äô) than live in uncongenial circumstances. ‚Ü©
  44. Eurystheus: The cowardly king who dispatched Heracles on twelve labours from the safety of his palace. ‚Ü©
  45. you are not even Theseus... troubles: Theseus was Athens‚Äô hometown hero, whose exploits were modelled on Heracles‚Äô, but confined to the area around Athens called Attica. ‚Ü©
  46. Procrustes or Sciron: Two robbers killed by Heracles. ‚Ü©
  47. the orator Theopompus... every little thing: Epictetus refers to Theopompus, the fourth-century BC historian, as an ‚Äòorator‚Äô because his writing reflects the training in rhetoric he received at the feet of Isocrates, from whom he also absorbed his bias against Plato; Isocrates‚Äô philosophical differences with Plato are laid out in his speech Against the Sophists. ‚Ü©
  48. impulse... appropriate acts: Cf. I 4, 11‚Äì12. ‚Ü©
  49. It is just this that Medea... dashed: The brilliant psychological handling of the Medea legend in Euripides‚Äô play was invoked by later philosophers in support of their own theories of moral misguidance; Epictetus treats her case in greater detail at I 28, 7. The following monologue is loosely based on lines 790ff. of the play. ‚Ü©
  50. Give me one student... delirious: Cf. I 18, 23 for the putative perfectibility of the mind implied in Epictetus‚Äô third field of study, which correlates with the traditional description of the Stoic sage. ‚Ü©
  51. the Liar: One of the most notorious of the ‚Äòlogic problems‚Äô alluded to above in ¬ß3. Cf. Cicero, Academica II 92‚Äì6: ‚ÄòIf you say that you are lying, and say so truly, are you lying or telling the truth?... If you say that you are lying, and you say so truly, you are lying. But you say that you are lying, and you say so truly. Therefore you are lying.‚Äô Epictetus references the Liar paradox again at II 18, 18 and II 21, 17. ‚Ü©
  52. Antisthenes: On Antisthenes, one of the foundational figures of Cynicism, cf. I 17, 12. ‚Ü©
  53. Antipater: This is Antipater of Tarsus. ‚Ü©
  54. the Master Argument itself: On the Master Argument, cf. II 19, 1‚Äì9. ‚Ü©
  55. the Quiescent: Cf. Sextus Empiricus, Against the Professors VII 416: ‚ÄòSince in the sorites the last cognitive impression is adjacent to the first non-cognitive impression and virtually indistinguishable from it, the school of Chrysippus say that in the case of impressions which differ so little the wise man will stop and become quiescent.‚Äô ‚Ü©
  56. It will even do... the past: Cf. Plato, Laws 854b. ‚Ü©
  57. how he lay... youthful beauty: The reference is to Plato‚Äôs Symposium 218d sq. ‚Ü©
  58. an Olympic-sized victory... Heracles: Heracles was the mythical founder of, and first victor in, the Olympic games. ‚Ü©
  59. pancratiasts: The pancration was an Olympic contest that combined elements of both boxing and wrestling. ‚Ü©
  60. the Dioscuri: The Dioscuri, Castor and Pollux, were patron deities of sailors and seafarers generally. ‚Ü©
  61. Hesiod‚Äôs verse... ever after: Hesiod was a seventh-century Greek poet; the quotation is from his poem Works and Days, line 413. ‚Ü©
  62. The Master Argument: This is the primary surviving reference to what Diodorus Cronus (for reasons unknown) named the ‚ÄòMaster Argument‚Äô. It owes something to the discussion of ‚ÄòThe Sea Battle‚Äô in chapter 9 of Aristotle‚Äôs De Interpretatione. Stoics (and their opponents) explored the implications of the argument for free will and personal responsibility, and their place (if any) within a determinist system like the Stoics‚Äô own. This probably also explains Epictetus‚Äô interest in it.

    First, a precis of Aristotle‚Äôs ‚ÄòSea Battle‚Äô. Either there will be a sea battle tomorrow, or there will not be. Only one of these statements can be true, the other must be false. But if it is now true that there will not be a sea battle tomorrow, then one might be led to infer that there is nothing one could do between now and tomorrow to bring about a sea battle ‚Äì even supposing that one were king of the country, say, or either of the fleet commanders.

    Diodorus‚Äô ‚ÄòMaster Argument‚Äô seems to have drawn on or developed this paradox insofar as it also engages the question of the truth value of statements about the future, and the deterministic implications of that question. But what Epictetus gives us is not Diodorus‚Äô argument ‚Äì or any argument, for that matter, in the sense of a set of premises yielding a conclusion ‚Äì but instead three propositions that he declares to be irreconcilable. His decision to present the Master Argument in this form has complicated efforts to reconstruct Diodorus‚Äô original, and distinguish it from rival counter- arguments. It would take us too far afield to analyse the Master Argument and its new modal language of possibility and necessity by adducing sources that complement Epictetus here. A tentative reconstruction of the Master Argument along the following lines, however, drawing on the present passage and continuing to use Aristotle‚Äôs example of the sea battle, may be useful for readers interested in pursuing the subject further.

    If a sea battle will not be fought tomorrow, then it was also true in the past that it would not be fought. And since (by principle 1 in Epictetus‚Äô text) everything past that is true is necessary, it was necessarily the case in the past that the battle would not take place, and necessarily false that it would take place. Thus, it was impossible that the sea battle would take place. And since (by principle 2) an impossibility cannot follow a possibility, it was impossible from the first moment of time (or from eternity) that the sea battle would take place. Therefore, if something will not be the case, it is never possible for it to be the case. And thus principle 3 in the text is proven false, and shown to be logically incompatible with the other two propositions. The conclusion that remains is that nothing, in fact, is possible which is not either true, or going to be true. ‚Ü©
  63. Antipater: This is Antipater of Tarsus. ‚Ü©
  64. Look, I wasn‚Äôt born... subject: The sequel shows that these replies are meant as satire and that Epictetus disapproves of the purely scholastic interest in the controversy that they evince. ‚Ü©
  65. Hellanicus: (c.480-395 BC), a writer on history, myth and ethnography. ‚Ü©
  66. you will tell us all about... ever will: Epictetus plays on the terms of the third principle in the Master Argument, above. ‚Ü©
  67. ‚ÄòThe wind... Ciconians‚Äô: Homer, Odyssey IX 39. ‚Ü©
  68. Diogenes: This is Diogenes of Babylon, head of the Stoic school in the early to mid-second century BC. ‚Ü©
  69. Peripatetics: The Peripatetics were the followers of Aristotle. Unlike the Stoics, Aristotle and his school taught that some portion of what Epictetus calls ‚Äòexternals‚Äô or ‚Äòindifferents‚Äô (such as health or social status) were indispensable for happiness. ‚Ü©
  70. you cannot be certain of anything: On the Sceptic philosophy of the Academics see I 5, and further below. ‚Ü©
  71. Epicurus is the same way... distinction: This passage tries to convict Epicurus of self-refutation, as the preceding passage aimed to do for the Sceptic philosophy of the New Academy. The claim that scepticism is internally incoherent made along the lines laid out in ¬ß¬ß1-5, was not new; Aristotle already cites and discusses the problem in Metaphysics III 5. A different kind of self-refutation argument, also aimed at the Academics, is used below, in ¬ß¬ß28-29: the Sceptics refute their doctrines in the process of performing life‚Äôs most basic tasks. This resembles the argument levelled at Epicurus here, in that he is shown negating his doctrines by the way he acts ‚Äì indeed, by the act of writing and publishing his doctrines. The close relation of the things that are supposed to be mutually incompatible brings the argument close in kind to the first one made against the Academics; accusations of self-refutation in this form were supposed to have particular force.

    A simpler form of the argument in ¬ß¬ß 6-11 is used in I 23. This one is more involved, and requires some explanation. In the view of Epictetus, Epicurus‚Äô social philosophy amounts to a kind of nihilism which, if enforced, would loose a free-for-all in which his followers would be as much at risk as the rest; so that, (Epictetus facetiously implies) if Epicurus were intelligent he would have allowed the mass of men to be neutralized by exposure to the social teachings of the Stoics, and only shared the truth with his disciples, who would then be better prepared for the state of war that naturally prevails among men, and therefore be in a better position to impose their will on the ‚Äòsheep‚Äô, who are ready to be ‚Äòshorn and milked‚Äô.

    Epictetus is not entirely unfair to Epicurus in representing his philosophy this way; as another Epicurean philosopher put it, ‚Äò[Without sound government] we would live a life of beasts, and one man on meeting another would all but devour him‚Äô (Colotes, third- century BC, in Plutarch, Against Colotes 1124D). By deliberately omitting any reference to ‚Äòsound government‚Äô, however, i.e. to the Epicurean social contract, he might be charged with only giving half the story. Be that as it may, Epictetus does correctly imply, in the last section (¬ß14), that, according to Epicurus again, men historically have been selective in who they make peace with, i.e. social relations are made on an ad hoc basis and for motives of self- interest ‚Äì or, as Epictetus puts it, they ‚Äòmake... a distinction‚Äô: ‚Äòsociability should be extended to some, but not others‚Äô. ‚Ü©
  72. We hear of Orestes... irresistible: The myth involving Orestes‚Äô murder of his mother, and subsequent persecution by the allegorized figures of Furies and Avengers, is best known from Aeschylus‚Äô dramatic trilogy, The Oresteia. Cybele was an agricultural deity whose priests in their drunken frenzy went so far as to castrate themselves. Epictetus implies that Epicurus‚Äô doctrines amount to sanctioning murder of the worst sort, such as Orestes‚Äô matricide, and encouraging drunken abandon, as represented by the behaviour of Cybele‚Äôs priests. ‚Ü©
  73. here come the refutations: The following passage is meant to illustrate the Academics‚Äô ability to argue either side of any argument, a practice that played an integral part in their Sceptic attitude, ‚Äòin order to show that their [opponents] had no certain or firm arguments about [any subject]‚Äô (Lactantius, Divine Institutes V 14, 5). ‚Ü©
  74. slavery is no more bad than good... quit their city?: Thermopylae was the site of one of the battles in the Persian War, which was also the occasion for the Athenians‚Äô twice evacuating their city (490-479 BC). ‚ÄòNo more this than that‚Äô was a formula of Sceptic epistemology, which Epictetus mimics above, and again in ¬ß30 below. ‚Ü©
  75. a Demeter, a Persephone, or a Pluto: Demeter was the Greek goddess of agriculture generally, and of grain in particular; Persephone was her daughter, Pluto (= Hades) was Persephone‚Äôs husband. Academics argued from the doubtful deity of minor gods notes like Persephone to challenge belief in the existence of major gods like Demeter. Epictetus suggests that, since bread was the staff of life, and in traditional Greek religion was the gift of Demeter, the Sceptics‚Äô agnosticism is proof of ingratitude as well as deliberate obtuseness. ‚Ü©
  76. most will allow that they are liable to feel pity: Stoics stigmatized pity (like most emotions) as a fault of character; but I 28, 9 shows that exceptions were allowed. ‚Ü©
  77. ‚ÄòYou want to see the light... father does too?‚Äô: From a passage in Euripides‚Äô Alcestis (line 691) where Admetus is roundly refused his request of his elderly father, Pheres, that he die in his place. ‚Ü©
  78. Anyone seeing them... views on friendship: The reference is probably to the Epicureans, who held that pleasure is the foundation of friendship, and that friends are not chosen for themselves, but for the benefits they bring; cf. Epicurus, Vatican Sayings 23: ‚ÄòAll friendship is an intrinsic virtue, but it originates from benefiting.‚Äô In light of what is argued in ¬ß¬ß15-21 below, however, conceivably it could include Epictetus‚Äô own views. ‚Ü©
  79. ‚ÄòWhere before the tower... same desire‚Äô: From the Phoenissae of Euripides, lines 621-2. Eteocles and Polyneices battle for control of Thebes after their father, Oedipus, blinds himself. ‚Ü©
  80. his beloved: Hephaestion, often described as Alexander‚Äôs ‚Äòalter ego‚Äô, who died on campaign with him in 324 BC. ‚Ü©
  81. ‚ÄòHonour is nothing... approved‚Äô: Possibly an allusion to the statement of Epicurus that ‚ÄòI spit on honour if it does not involve pleasure‚Äô (H. Usener, Epicurea, Leipzig, 1887, p. 512). ‚Ü©
  82. And Eriphyle... came between them: Eriphyle was induced by the bribe of a necklace to get her husband to join Polyneices in his war on Eteocles for control of Thebes. ‚Ü©
  83. ‚ÄòEvery soul... will‚Äô: A paraphrase of Plato‚Äôs Sophist, 228c, quoted again at I 28, 4. ‚Ü©
  84. endowed them with breath... their shape: The Stoics explained vision as an active rather than a passive process, whereby the ‚Äòbreath‚Äô (pneuma) of which the soul is composed exits the eyes and apprehends objects by essentially modelling them. ‚Ü©
  85. Which part let your beard grow long?: A long beard was the badge of a philosopher. ‚Ü©
  86. Your goal was different... citizen yourself: A Greek citizen traditionally served a term in the army (‚Äòhelp insure the safety of the citizenry‚Äô) before settling down to civilian life. In urging his students to return to their native cities after studying under him, and deprecating the study of rhetoric, Epictetus evidently wanted to discourage his students from adopting the life of a travelling sophist, which was fashionable in his day (the movement collectively known as the ‚ÄòSecond Sophistic‚Äô). ‚Ü©
  87. ‚Äò‚ÄòLead me, Zeus... Destiny‚Äô: From Cleanthes‚Äô ‚ÄòHymn to Zeus‚Äô; cf. III 22, 95 and Enchiridion 53. ‚Ü©
  88. Demosthenes: A famous Athenian orator of the fourth century bc. ‚Ü©

Book 3

  1. another from within: An allusion to God, as at I 25, 13. ‚Ü©
  2. breath: ‚ÄòBreath‚Äô translates pneuma, one of the elements in Stoic physics and the substance of the human soul. Epictetus alludes to pneuma again, at II 23, 3. ‚Ü©
  3. The soul... normal also: This last paragraph is irrelevant here and probably displaced from its original context. We find other fragments of this kind appended to the end of III 8, 7. ‚Ü©
  4. Nemean, Pythian, Isthmian and Olympic Games conjointly: A list of the four Panhellenic festivals. ‚Ü©
  5. One person... self-improvement: A paraphrase of Xenophon, Memorabilia I 6, 8 sq., or Plato, Protagoras 318a. ‚Ü©
  6. These are the things... Protagoras or Hippias: Protagoras and Hippias were prominent teachers of rhetoric, or ‚ÄòSophists‚Äô, contemporaneous with Socrates in Athens. This passage is influenced by two traditions concerning Socrates: 1) that he was the first philosopher devoted primarily to the pursuit of knowledge about ethical as opposed to physical questions; and 2) that he simultaneously claimed to know nothing except that he knew nothing. He can be found escorting young men to Protagoras and Hippias, respectively, at Plato, Protagoras 310e and Theaetetus 151b. ‚Ü©
  7. cognitive impression: (Phantasia kataleÀÜptikeÀÜ), the centrepiece of Stoic epistemology: an impression so clear and accurate that there could be no doubt as to its veracity. ‚Ü©
  8. If you care to know... like him: That the Romans had a low opinion of philosophers, and believed philosophy was a waste of time at best, at worst a brand of charlatanry, is a well-known half-truth; but as the otherwise unknown Italicus is described as ‚Äòone of [Rome‚Äôs] finest philosophers‚Äô, his remark is unlikely to reflect that attitude. Clearly he intends no compliment, however, in protesting that his friends ‚Äòwant me to end up like [Epictetus]‚Äô. It may be that Romans by this time were so corrupted by materialism that no one who lived as simply and frugally as Epictetus could be considered a success at anything, philosophy included; by this time they had the example of Seneca before them ‚Äì both fabulously rich and the first philosopher of his day. If more were known about this Italicus (and it is possible that he and the whole anecdote are an invention), all might be made clear, but as it stands the passage is obscure. ‚Ü©
  9. It was no small advantage... after all: In Greek myth, Menoeceus sacrificed his life to save his native Thebes. ‚Ü©
  10. the father of Admetus: Pheres, by name, who in Euripides‚Äô play Alcestis refuses to die in his son‚Äôs place despite his advanced age. ‚Ü©
  11. I bless Lesbios... nothing: Lesbios is otherwise unknown. ‚Ü©
  12. On Cynicism: Cynicism was a school of philosophy founded in the fourth century BC by Diogenes of Sinope. Like the Stoics, Cynics taught that the key to happiness was living in agreement with nature. To a unique degree, however, Cynics emphasized the need for training (Greek askeÀÜsis), both mental and physical, to achieve this end ‚Äì thus the stories about Diogenes walking barefoot in the snow, embracing marble statues naked in winter and so on. From Diogenes‚Äô point of view, the fewer artifacts and less technology he needed for survival, the more his life was in ‚Äòagreement‚Äô with nature. For this reason, too, his only shelter, supposedly, was an empty wine vat (or ‚Äòtub‚Äô). Although he is said to have imitated animals in systematically ridding himself of inessentials, he believed that it was not enough in man‚Äôs case to reduce one‚Äôs possessions to the minimum; human beings also had to live virtuously. Indeed, one point of shedding inessentials was to prove decisively that virtue alone, without the need of material goods, was enough to secure human happiness. Thus the negative side of Cynicism, its primitivism, was subordinate to this positive aspect, the belief it shared with the Stoics that virtue was man‚Äôs end and a necessary and sufficient condition for his happiness.

    Stoics called Cynicism ‚Äòa shortcut to virtue‚Äô (Diogenes Laertius, The Lives of the Philosophers VII 121). Implicit in this description is not only the Cynics‚Äô ethical absolutism but also their rejection of physics and logic (ibid. VI 103). Cynicism enjoyed a revival in the first century ad owing in part to the fact that their message resonated in a society (Rome) that regretted the loss of the virtues and values that originally made her great, in favour of materialism, hedonism and duplicity. What the Romans could not accept or imitate was the shameless side of Cynicism: the ‚Äòdog‚Äô side (Cynic means ‚Äòdog-like‚Äô), that is, their sometimes outrageous lack of dignity (cf. ¬ß80 below). Epictetus does his part here to rescue Cynicism for his Roman audience by replacing anaideia, a byword of early Cynicism meaning ‚Äòshamelessness‚Äô or ‚Äòbrazenness‚Äô, with its opposite, aidos, ‚Äòdignity‚Äô, calling it the Cynic‚Äôs proper ‚Äòprotection‚Äô. And whereas the Stoic sage was a remote abstraction, the Cynic, the embodiment of virtue, was a very public figure, a man of the streets ‚Äì devoted to showing others by his example that happiness was compatible with a life lived at the limits of purity and privation. The Cynic was avowedly extreme; but by demonstrating in tangible terms that such contempt for wealth, luxury and fame as he evinced was feasible, he also showed that loss of these things was nothing to fear: he was a living incitement to ataraxia: ‚Äòserenity‚Äô, ‚Äòpeace of mind‚Äô. As Seneca writes, Diogenes proved that nature imposes nothing that is impossible to bear (Letters 90. 14). Thus, the Cynic was for the Romans an inspiration as much as a realistic model for imitation. It is noteworthy that Epictetus in this Discourse has nothing good to say about the Cynics of his own day and spends more time discouraging rather than recruiting for the Cynic vocation. This may be just a test of the seriousness of one‚Äôs commitment to the cause. But overall the Discourse is less a protreptic, that is, an exhortation to Cynic philosophy, than a doctrinal study of Cynicism, and an extended encomium of Diogenes, the ethical model cited most often in the Discourses, Socrates only excepted. ‚Ü©
  13. he is also a spy: The Cynic is compared to a ‚Äòspy‚Äô in several ancient sources. As a spy abandons the safety of his own camp and risks solitary incursion into enemy territory, relying on darkness and his own wits to survive, so the Cynic eschews the support of a family and the security of four walls and a roof in favour of a solitary and itinerant lifestyle among an enemy of his own creation, the homme moyen sensuel, whom he aims to shock and ridicule with his brashness and his outrageous theatrics. By calling him a ‚Äòspy‚Äô Epictetus also implies that the Cynic ideally plies his trade in the service of others, not for selfish or personal reasons. He ‚Äòspies out‚Äô the conditions of life that most men fear ‚Äì poverty, austerity, exile, social ignominy ‚Äì and reports ‚Äì just by the example of his freedom, his good spirits and his wit ‚Äì that far from being things to fear, and worth compromising one‚Äôs integrity in order to avoid, these conditions comprise life nearer to, and therefore more like, the life nature meant for us to lead. ‚Ü©
  14. He needs to be like Diogenes... Philip: On this incident cf. I 24, 3- 10 (above), and Diogenes Laertius, The Lives of the Philosophers VI 43. ‚Ü©
  15. look at Myron or Ophellius... which they are not: Myron and Ophellius were contemporary athletes or gladiators ‚Äì and presumably came to a bad end like Croesus. Reference to the plight of ‚Äòthe rich of today‚Äô may allude to the practice of the first emperors of charging prominent citizens with treason (maiestas), with the covert goal of appropriating their assets for themselves. These paradoxical unfortunates may overlap with the statesmen who drew unwelcome attention to themselves by holding more than one consulship. ‚Ü©
  16. Sardanapalus: The last king of Babylon, and proverbial for luxury. ‚Ü©
  17. He pulled many a hair... my chest: The quotes relating to Agamemnon are from the Iliad 10. 15, 91, and 94-5. ‚Ü©
  18. You are rich in gold and bronze: Epictetus quotes Iliad 18. 289. ‚Ü©
  19. identify the Cynic... leaves bare: A Cynic characteristically wore his signature rough mantle (the tribon) with one shoulder exposed; this helps, for instance, to identify Diogenes in Raphael‚Äôs painting The School of Athens. Sarcastic reference to ‚Äòlarge jaws‚Äô evokes the popular reputation of the Cynic as a parasitic glutton, a malicious parody of his true nature for which the writers of comedy were no doubt responsible. ‚Ü©
  20. Take me to the proconsul at once: The proconsul functioned as a judge in the provinces of the Roman Empire. ‚Ü©
  21. the Great King: The common designation for the king of the Persian empire, proverbially the most fortunate person on earth. ‚Ü©
  22. the sceptre and the kingdom: The Cynic‚Äôs signature staff is featured as a royal sceptre, in line with the paradoxical presentation of the Cynic as the true king; cf. ¬ß34 above. At the same time he is ‚Äòminister‚Äô to Zeus, who sent him to rule among men; cf. ¬ß69 below. ‚Ü©
  23. Friendship... indefinitely: As the Cynic was lampooned in ancient comedy as a parasite, so Epictetus represents parasitism as antithetical to the Cynic ideal of independence. ‚Ü©
  24. who has the people... concerns: Homer, Iliad 2. 25 ‚Ü©
  25. his children will not be Cynics straight out of the womb: I.e. they are not yet ready to survive on the Cynic‚Äôs meagre regimen; they still have various material needs. ‚Ü©
  26. Crates had a wife: Crates‚Äô wife was named Hipparchia. Crates made adoption of his philosophy a condition of their marriage, and out of love for him she complied: Diogenes Laertius, The Lives of the Philosophers VI 96. ‚Ü©
  27. Epaminondas... Priam... Danaus, or Aeolus: Epaminondas was a fourth-century BC Theban general who scored several notable victories over Sparta. Priam, Danaus and Aeolus are figures from the Greek epic cycle, which is why they are contrasted here with Homer, their chronicler. Like Priam, Danaus and Aeolus were mythical kings credited with epic-sized families; Homer, according to tradition, lacked both home and family. ‚Ü©
  28. dogs who beg... gate: Iliad 22. 69; the word ‚ÄòCynic‚Äô comes from the Greek word for dog. ‚Ü©
  29. A man charged... concerns: Homer, Iliad 2. 24‚Äì5; cf. ¬ß72 above. Diogenes‚Äô ability to give the next line of Homer‚Äôs text and finish Alexander‚Äôs thought instantaneously shows, in fact, that he was not ‚Äòsleeping the night away‚Äô but even when sleeping was actually half- awake ‚Äì as Homer said a leader of men ought to be. ‚Ü©
  30. what sleep he gets... lay down: I.e. his reason does not desert him even when asleep; so that, unlike most people, he is not prey to indecent dreams. Cf. I 18, 23 on the dangers posed by sleep, madness and intoxication. ‚Ü©
  31. make Argus seem blind by comparison: Argus was a mythical creature with many eyes. ‚Ü©
  32. No, go inside... in particular: Homer, Iliad 6. 490 sq., partly paraphrased. ‚Ü©
  33. The one where I described Pan and the Nymphs: Evidently a reference to ekphrasis, description of a work of art, and a staple of epideictic oratory, which flourished when other types of oratory dried up after the establishment of the Principate, parallelling the history of rhetoric in Greece following Alexander the Great. ‚Ü©
  34. Dio: Possibly Dio Chrysostom, a contemporary of Epictetus whose writings represent some of the most successful blends in Greek literature of rhetoric and philosophy. ‚Ü©
  35. Since this man is constant... does the constant man have?: A typically Socratic move to define a particular virtue, in this case, constancy. ‚Ü©
  36. Lysias or Isocrates: Leading rhetoricians in classical Athens. ‚Ü©
  37. I often wondered by what sort of arguments: The opening words of the Memorabilia, Xenophon‚Äôs account of Socrates‚Äô life and teaching. ‚Ü©
  38. ‚ÄòAnytus and Meletus... cannot harm me‚Äô: Socrates is quoted from Plato‚Äôs Apology 30c; G. Vlastos (Socrates: Ironist and Moral Philosopher, Cambridge University Press, 1991, p. 219) rightly calls this Epictetus‚Äô ‚Äòfavourite text‚Äô; cf. I 29, 18; II 2, 15, etc. ‚Ü©
  39. I‚Äôve always been the sort... my own affairs: The quote is from Plato‚Äôs Crito 46b; Socrates is the speaker. ‚Ü©
  40. ‚ÄòI know something and I teach it‚Äô: I.e. Socrates was unlike the Sophists in declining to teach and take money, one proof of his contempt for his personal affairs. It also reflects his ‚Äòirony‚Äô, i.e. his denial of any knowledge, hence having anything to teach. ‚Ü©
  41. ‚ÄòIt would not suit me... callow youth‚Äô: Plato, Apology 17c. ‚Ü©
  42. As the sun... need of help: On the sun in Stoic physics, cf. I 19, 11. ‚Ü©
  43. Rufus: Musonius Rufus, Epictetus‚Äô teacher. ‚Ü©
  44. the epideictic style... as a fourth subject: The epideictic, or display, style of rhetoric, is one of the three genres of oratory recognized by Aristotle in his Rhetoric, along with forensic and deliberative, though he limits its application to praise or denigration, as this was how it was actually applied in his day, when it was still a new form of rhetoric; a surviving example from around Aristotle‚Äôs day is Isocrates‚Äô Helen. The protreptic style was developed as a means to encourage devotion to philosophy; Aristotle himself composed an influential early example, as did Stoics including Cleanthes. The elenctic style is not classified separately by Aristotle, although he does discuss refutational arguments (Rhetoric 1396b25). The didactic style was presumably adapted for teaching; we know no more of it per se. By implying that the protreptic style should not be classified alongside the others, Epictetus presumably means that it is out of place in a philosophical curriculum. ‚Ü©

Book 4

  1. ‚ÄòBy the grace of Caesar, we are free‚Äô: I.e. the form of the expression makes it self-contradictory. ‚ÄòFree‚Äô in this context means politically autonomous and exempt from Roman taxation. ‚Ü©
  2. Thrasonides, who fought more campaigns, perhaps, than you: Thrasonides is the soldier-protagonist of the Misoumenos, a play by the fourth-century BC Greek comic playwright Menander; his pet slave was Geta. ‚ÄòCampaigns‚Äô here puns on a secondary meaning of the word, to refer to love affairs, a usage derived from the language of Roman elegy. ‚Ü©
  3. the five per cent tax: In Rome the five per cent tax was levied on holders of high public office, the assumption being that the slave aspires to be free so that he will qualify to hold such office. ‚Ü©
  4. a ring on my finger... blissful and complete: Wearing a gold ring was the sign and privilege of the Roman equestrian (or ‚Äòupper-middle‚Äô) class. ‚Ü©
  5. he joins the army... third tour of duty: Cf. II 14, 17: By a law of Julius Caesar, service in at least three campaigns was required for eligibility to become a municipal senator. ‚Ü©
  6. the inability to apply common preconceptions to particulars: On this topic, cf. esp. II 17. ‚Ü©
  7. Caesar‚Äôs friend: On the semi-official status of being ‚ÄòCaesar‚Äôs friend‚Äô, see F. Millar, The Emperor in the Roman World (Duckworth, 1977), pp. 110-22. ‚Ü©
  8. buying, selling and other such mundane transactions: I.e. buying and selling slaves, which has been declared irrelevant to the question of what true freedom consists in. ‚Ü©
  9. twelve bodyguards: Literally, ‚Äòtwelve fasces,‚Äô a bundle of rods bound together around an axe with the blade projecting, carried before Roman magistrates as an emblem of their authority, by lictors, a magistrate‚Äôs usual escort. ‚Ü©
  10. a slave on holiday at the Saturnalia: At Saturnalia restrictions were relaxed and slaves were treated as equals. Thus ‚Äòa slave on holiday at the Saturnalia‚Äô means someone enjoying mock, provisional freedom; cf. I 25, 8. ‚Ü©
  11. we even honour them as gods... false as well: The argument runs: whatever can confer the most benet is a god. But this man (i.e. the emperor) has the power to confer the most benefit. Therefore the emperor is a god. The second (minor) premise is erroneous, resulting in a false conclusion. The emperors were honoured as deities after their death; the cult of Augustus Caesar was prominent in Epictetus‚Äô hometown of Nicopolis. ‚Ü©
  12. How is a fortress demolished?: The ‚Äòfortress‚Äô (Greek: acropolis) is a metaphor for the mind or, in Stoic parlance, for the ‚Äòruling principle‚Äô (hegemonikon). The use of ‚Äòfortress‚Äô to represent the mind (or soul) goes back to Plato (Republic 560b, Timaeus 70a); it is also used by Marcus Aurelius in this connection (Meditations Book 8-48). ‚Ü©
  13. quaestor: A Roman official in charge of overseeing provincial finances. ‚Ü©
  14. a slave presenting your emancipator: A slave had to present his emancipator before a judge as part of the rite of manumission. ‚Ü©
  15. Which explains his behaviour... captive: An allusion to a famous incident in the life of Diogenes the Cynic, illustrative of how freedom is not a physical condition but a virtue of character and a state of mind. In his old age, sailing to Aegina, he was taken by pirates and carried to Crete, where he was put on the slave market. When the auctioneer asked him what he could do, he said, ‚ÄòI can govern men; so sell me to someone who needs a master.‚Äô ‚Ü©
  16. I continue to value... healthy at all: Epictetus alludes to his lameness. ‚Ü©
  17. Diogenes: Diogenes the Cynic (again) as in ¬ß114 above; he is presented here as a citizen of the world; the entire world is his ‚Äòcountry‚Äô. ‚Ü©
  18. the Persian king... Archidamus, king of the Spartans: Personages representing the acme of power from the Greek and barbarian worlds, respectively, together suggestive of Diogenes‚Äô ‚Äòcosmopolitan‚Äô outlook. ‚Ü©
  19. the law: The universal or divine law. ‚Ü©
  20. Socrates... obey it: The events in Socrates‚Äô life rehearsed in this section are taken from the account given in Plato‚Äôs Apology. ‚Ü©
  21. In his own words... injustice: A paraphrase of Plato, Crito 47d; the ‚Äòelement‚Äô is the soul. ‚Ü©
  22. resisted the Athenians‚Äô call... vote: The motion to execute the generals who commanded at the Battle of Arginusae in 406 BC, an episode in the Peloponnesian War. ‚Ü©
  23. as Plato says: Phaedo 64a, 67d-e. ‚Ü©
  24. the Palatine: The hill in Rome where the emperors had their palaces. ‚Ü©
  25. Cassius and Masurius: Noted jurists active in the first half of the first century AD. ‚Ü©
  26. the same man... prison walls: Cf. Plato, Phaedo 6od, where Socrates is reported to have composed a hymn to Apollo while awaiting execution in jail. ‚Ü©
  27. A soldier in civilian dress... chains: This probably refers to methods employed during the emperor Domitian‚Äôs reign of terror in the early 90s AD, when Epictetus was still in Rome. ‚Ü©
